- Arc length $= \frac{\theta}{2\pi} \cdot 2\pi r = r\theta$
- Sector area $= \frac{\theta}{2\pi} \cdot \pi r^2 = \frac{1}{2}r^2\theta$

**Critical distinction**: Arc length is a *length* (linear units). Sector area is an *area* (square units). They are not the same type of quantity. The SAT sometimes includes answer choices that confuse the two — students who memorize one formula may accidentally apply it in the wrong context.

### 6.2.3 Inscribed Angles vs. Central Angles

One of the most important and frequently tested relationships in circle geometry is the **inscribed angle theorem**:

> An inscribed angle measures **half** the measure of its intercepted arc.

Since a central angle measuring $\theta$ intercepts an arc of $\theta$ degrees, an inscribed angle intercepting the **same arc** measures $\frac{\theta}{2}$.

This theorem generates several powerful consequences:

1. **Inscribed angle subtended by a diameter**: If an inscribed angle intercepts a semicircle (an arc of $180^\circ$), the angle measures $90^\circ$. This is the famous "inscribed in a semicircle" right angle. The SAT loves this — whenever you see a triangle inscribed in a circle with one side as the diameter, you immediately know it is a right triangle.

2. **Inscribed angles intercepting the same arc are congruent.** If two (or more) inscribed angles share the same intercepted arc, they have the same measure.

3. **A quadrilateral inscribed in a circle (cyclic quadrilateral) has opposite angles that sum to $180^\circ$**. This follows directly from the inscribed angle theorem: together, two opposite inscribed angles intercept the full $360^\circ$ of the circle, so their measures sum to $180^\circ$.

### 6.2.4 The Equation of a Circle

The SAT references the **standard form** of a circle equation:

$$(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

where $(h, k)$ is the center and $r$ is the radius.

**Key observations for SAT problem-solving:**

- $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ has center $(h, k)$, **not** $(-h, -k)$. The signs are "flipped" because the equation is set equal to zero conceptually.
- $r^2$ is the squared radius on the right side of the equation, not the radius itself. To get the radius, take the square root of the right-hand side.
- A circle with center at the origin has equation $x^2 + y^2 = r^2$.

**Expanded (general) form:** $x^2 + y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0$

To convert from expanded form to standard form, you must **complete the square** for both the $x$ and $y$ terms. This is a standard SAT skill that ties your geometry and algebra knowledge together.

**Example:** Convert $x^2 + y^2 - 6x + 4y - 12 = 0$ to standard form.
- Group $x$ terms and $y$ terms: $(x^2 - 6x) + (y^2 + 4y) = 12$
- Complete the square: $(x^2 - 6x + 9) + (y^2 + 4y + 4) = 12 + 9 + 4$
- Factor: $(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 = 25$
- The center is $(3, -2)$ and the radius is $\sqrt{25} = 5$.

**SAT strategy**: When you see a circle equation in an SAT problem, your first move should almost always be to identify the center and radius. Even questions that appear purely algebraic (e.g., "what is the area of the circle?") are actually geometry questions in disguise.

### 6.2.5 Radians and Degrees

The Digital SAT sometimes uses radians to measure angles. You must be comfortable converting between the two systems:

$$180^\circ = \pi \text{ radians}$$

- Degrees to radians: multiply by $\frac{\pi}{180^\circ}$
- Radians to degrees: multiply by $\frac{180^\circ}{\pi}$

| Degrees | Radians |
|---|---|
| $30^\circ$ | $\frac{\pi}{6}$ |
| $45^\circ$ | $\frac{\pi}{4}$ |
| $60^\circ$ | $\frac{\pi}{3}$ |
| $90^\circ$ | $\frac{\pi}{2}$ |
| $120^\circ$ | $\frac{2\pi}{3}$ |
| $135^\circ$ | $\frac{3\pi}{4}$ |
| $150^\circ$ | $\frac{5\pi}{6}$ |
| $180^\circ$ | $\pi$ |
| $270^\circ$ | $\frac{3\pi}{2}$ |
| $360^\circ$ | $2\pi$ |

**Desmos tip**: The Desmos calculator in Bluebook defaults to **degrees**. If a problem involves radians, you must change the angle mode by clicking the wrench icon in the top-right corner and switching to radians. If you forget, your trig values will be wrong. Make it a habit to check the mode before any trig problem.

### 6.2.6 Tangent Lines

The **tangent line to a circle** at a given point is perpendicular to the radius drawn to that point of tangency. This creates a right angle at the point of tangency, which means:

1. If a tangent line problem involves finding a distance or length, you can often construct a right triangle involving the radius and use the Pythagorean theorem.
2. Two tangent segments drawn from the **same external point** to a circle are always congruent. This is a worthwhile geometric fact that the SAT occasionally tests.
3. When a line is tangent to a circle, the perpendicular from the center of the circle to that line passes through the point of tangency.

### 6.2.7 Circle Problem-Solving Flowchart

When you encounter a circle question on the SAT, apply this decision framework:

1. **Is the question asking about area or circumference?** Use $A = \pi r^2$ or $C = 2\pi r$.
2. **Is the question asking about an arc or sector?** Use the proportional formulas: $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times 2\pi r$ for arc length, $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times \pi r^2$ for sector area.
3. **Is the question about angles?** Determine whether the angle is central, inscribed, or formed by intersecting chords/secants/tangents. Apply the inscribed angle theorem or the angle-sum properties.
4. **Is the question in the coordinate plane?** Use $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$. Identify center and radius. Complete the square if needed. Determine whether a point lies inside, on, or outside the circle by comparing $(x_p - h)^2 + (y_p - k)^2$ to $r^2$.
5. **Is a right angle involved?** Check for the inscribed angle in a semicircle or a tangent-radius relationship.

---

## 6.3 Polygons — Angle Sums, Regular Polygons, and Properties

### 6.3.1 Sum of Interior Angles

Any polygon with $n$ sides can be divided into $(n - 2)$ triangles. Since each triangle has $180^\circ$, the sum of the interior angles is:

$$\text{Sum of interior angles} = (n - 2) \times 180^\circ$$

This formula is **not** on the SAT reference sheet and must be memorized.

| Polygon | $n$ | Sum of Interior Angles |
|---|---|---|
| Triangle | 3 | $180^\circ$ |
| Quadrilateral | 4 | $360^\circ$ |
| Pentagon | 5 | $540^\circ$ |
| Hexagon | 6 | $720^\circ$ |
| Heptagon | 7 | $900^\circ$ |
| Octagon | 8 | $1080^\circ$ |
| Decagon | 10 | $1440^\circ$ |

### 6.3.2 Sum of Exterior Angles

The sum of the exterior angles of **any** convex polygon, one at each vertex, is always $360^\circ$. This is true regardless of the number of sides. This is a powerful fact because it means:

- For a **regular** polygon (all sides and angles equal), each exterior angle $= \frac{360^\circ}{n}$.
- Each interior angle of a regular polygon $= \frac{(n-2) \times 180^\circ}{n}$, which also equals $180^\circ - \text{exterior angle}$.

### 6.3.3 Regular Polygons

A **regular polygon** has all sides congruent and all interior angles congruent. The SAT most commonly tests regular hexagons, regular octagons, and regular pentagons.

For a regular $n$-gon:
- Each interior angle $= \frac{(n-2) \times 180^\circ}{n}$
- Each exterior angle $= \frac{360^\circ}{n}$
- Interior angle + exterior angle $= 180^\circ$ (they are supplementary)

**Regular hexagon ($n = 6$):**
- Each interior angle $= \frac{4 \times 180^\circ}{6} = 120^\circ$
- Each exterior angle $= \frac{360^\circ}{6} = 60^\circ$
- A regular hexagon can be divided into 6 equilateral triangles by drawing lines from the center to each vertex. This is a useful decomposition for area problems.

**Regular octagon ($n = 8$):**
- Each interior angle $= \frac{6 \times 180^\circ}{8} = 135^\circ$
- Each exterior angle $= \frac{360^\circ}{8} = 45^\circ$

### 6.3.4 Diagonals of a Polygon

The number of diagonals in an $n$-sided polygon is:

$$\text{Diagonals} = \frac{n(n-3)}{2}$$

This formula is occasionally useful on the SAT, though it is not directly tested as often as angle sums. The reasoning: from each vertex, you can draw a diagonal to $(n - 3)$ other vertices (excluding itself and its two adjacent vertices). Multiply by $n$ vertices and divide by 2 to avoid double-counting.

### 6.3.5 Polygon Problem-Solving Strategies

1. **When you see a polygon with an unknown angle**, use the interior angle sum formula to set up an equation.
2. **When you see a regular polygon**, immediately compute the interior and exterior angles. These are your building blocks.
3. **When a polygon is inscribed in a circle**, the vertices lie on the circle. Use circle theorems (inscribed angle theorem) in combination with polygon angle properties.
4. **When a polygon is divided into triangles**, use the fact that each triangle contributes $180^\circ$ to the total angle sum.

---

## 6.4 Quadrilaterals — Classification, Properties, and Area

### 6.4.1 The Quadrilateral Family Tree

Quadrilaterals form a hierarchy based on their properties. Understanding this hierarchy is essential because the SAT often asks questions that require you to recognize which properties apply to which shapes.

```
Quadrilateral (4 sides, sum of interior angles = 360°)
│
├── Trapezoid (at least one pair of parallel sides)
│   │
│   └── Isosceles Trapezoid (non-parallel sides congruent; base angles congruent)
│
└── Parallelogram (both pairs of opposite sides parallel)
    │
    ├── Rectangle (parallelogram with 4 right angles)
    │   │
    │   └── Square (rectangle with 4 equal sides)
    │
    └── Rhombus (parallelogram with 4 equal sides)
        │
        └── Square (rhombus with 4 right angles)
```

**Key insight**: A square is both a rectangle and a rhombus. It inherits all properties of both. This means any property that applies to rectangles or rhombuses also applies to squares.

### 6.4.2 Parallelogram Properties

A **parallelogram** is a quadrilateral with both pairs of opposite sides parallel. Its properties include:

1. Opposite sides are **congruent** (equal in length).
2. Opposite angles are **congruent**.
3. Consecutive angles are **supplementary** (sum to $180^\circ$).
4. Diagonals **bisect each other** (they cut each other in half).
5. Diagonals do **not** necessarily bisect the angles (this is a property of rhombuses, not general parallelograms).

**Area of a parallelogram**: $A = bh$, where $b$ is the base and $h$ is the perpendicular height (not the slanted side length). This is a common SAT trap — students mistakenly use the slanted side as the height.

**Perimeter of a parallelogram**: $P = 2(b + s)$, where $s$ is the length of the slanted side.

### 6.4.3 Rectangle Properties

A **rectangle** is a parallelogram with four right angles. It inherits all parallelogram properties plus:

1. All four angles are $90^\circ$.
2. Diagonals are **congruent** (equal in length).
3. Diagonals do **not** necessarily bisect the angles (unless it is a square).

**Area**: $A = lw$ (length × width)

**Perimeter**: $P = 2(l + w)$

**Diagonal**: By the Pythagorean theorem, $d = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2}$

### 6.4.4 Rhombus Properties

A **rhombus** is a parallelogram with four congruent sides. It inherits all parallelogram properties plus:

1. All four sides are congruent.
2. Diagonals are **perpendicular** to each other.
3. Diagonals **bisect the interior angles**.
4. Diagonals are **not** necessarily congruent (unless it is a square).

**Area**: $A = bh$ (base × height, same as parallelogram)

**Alternative area formula using diagonals**: $A = \frac{1}{2}d_1 d_2$, where $d_1$ and $d_2$ are the lengths of the diagonals. This formula is **not** on the SAT reference sheet and must be memorized. It is extremely useful when the diagonals are given but the height is not.

### 6.4.5 Square Properties

A **square** is both a rectangle and a rhombus. It has the most restrictive set of properties:

1. All four sides are congruent.
2. All four angles are $90^\circ$.
3. Diagonals are congruent.
4. Diagonals are perpendicular.
5. Diagonals bisect the interior angles (each angle is bisected into $45^\circ$).

**Area**: $A = s^2$

**Perimeter**: $P = 4s$

**Diagonal**: $d = s\sqrt{2}$ (from the Pythagorean theorem: $\sqrt{s^2 + s^2} = s\sqrt{2}$)

**Relationship between diagonal and side**: $s = \frac{d}{\sqrt{2}} = \frac{d\sqrt{2}}{2}$

### 6.4.6 Trapezoid Properties

A **trapezoid** (trapezium in British English) is a quadrilateral with **at least one pair of parallel sides**. The parallel sides are called **bases** ($b_1$ and $b_2$), and the non-parallel sides are called **legs**.

**Area**: $A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h = \left(\frac{b_1 + b_2}{2}\right)h$

The quantity $\frac{b_1 + b_2}{2}$ is called the **median** (or midsegment) of the trapezoid. The area formula can be read as: **median × height**.

**Isosceles trapezoid**: A trapezoid with congruent legs. Properties include:
- Base angles are congruent.
- Diagonals are congruent.

### 6.4.7 Quadrilateral Problem-Solving Strategies

1. **Identify the most specific category** the shape belongs to. If it is a square, use square properties — not just parallelogram properties. The more specific the shape, the more tools you have.
2. **When area is needed but height is not given**, look for right triangles (Pythagorean theorem) or special right triangles (30-60-90, 45-45-90) to find the height.
3. **When diagonals are given for a rhombus**, use $A = \frac{1}{2}d_1 d_2$.
4. **When a quadrilateral is inscribed in a circle**, opposite angles sum to $180^\circ$.
5. **When coordinates are given**, use the distance formula, slope formula, and midpoint formula to verify properties (parallel sides have equal slopes, perpendicular sides have negative reciprocal slopes, congruent sides have equal lengths).

---

## 6.5 Solid Figures (3D Geometry) — Volume, Surface Area, and Scaling

### 6.5.1 Overview of 3D Figures on the SAT

Solid geometry typically appears in 1 to 2 questions per test, usually at the end of modules (higher difficulty). The most commonly tested solids are rectangular prisms, cubes, cylinders, and spheres. Cones and pyramids appear less frequently but are still within the test's scope.

**Formulas provided on the SAT reference sheet:**

| Solid | Volume |
|---|---|
| Rectangular solid | $V = lwh$ |
| Cylinder | $V = \pi r^2 h$ |
| Sphere | $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ |
| Cone | $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ |
| Pyramid | $V = \frac{1}{3}lwh$ |

**Formulas you must memorize:**

| Solid | Surface Area |
|---|---|
| Cube | $SA = 6s^2$ |
| Rectangular solid | $SA = 2lw + 2lh + 2wh$ |
| Cylinder | $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$ |
| Sphere | $SA = 4\pi r^2$ |
| Cone | $SA = \pi r^2 + \pi rl$ (where $l$ = slant height) |

**Additional formulas to memorize:**

| Formula | Expression |
|---|---|
| Diagonal of rectangular solid | $d = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2 + h^2}$ |
| Diagonal of a cube | $d = s\sqrt{3}$ |

### 6.5.2 Rectangular Prism (Rectangular Solid)

A rectangular prism has 6 rectangular faces, 12 edges, and 8 vertices.

- **Volume**: $V = lwh$
- **Surface Area**: $SA = 2lw + 2lh + 2wh$ (sum of areas of all 6 faces)
- **Space Diagonal**: $d = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2 + h^2}$ (the longest diagonal, running from one corner through the interior to the opposite corner)

The space diagonal formula is derived by applying the Pythagorean theorem twice: first to find the diagonal of the base ($\sqrt{l^2 + w^2}$), then using that as one leg of a right triangle whose hypotenuse is the space diagonal: $\sqrt{(\sqrt{l^2 + w^2})^2 + h^2} = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2 + h^2}$.

### 6.5.3 Cube

A cube is a special rectangular prism where all edges are equal ($l = w = h = s$).

- **Volume**: $V = s^3$
- **Surface Area**: $SA = 6s^2$ (6 faces, each with area $s^2$)
- **Space Diagonal**: $d = s\sqrt{3}$
- **Face Diagonal**: $d_{\text{face}} = s\sqrt{2}$

**Cube inscribed in a sphere**: The space diagonal of the cube equals the diameter of the sphere. If the cube has side $s$, the sphere has diameter $s\sqrt{3}$ and radius $\frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2}$.

**Sphere inscribed in a cube**: The diameter of the sphere equals the side of the cube. If the cube has side $s$, the sphere has radius $\frac{s}{2}$.

These inscribed/circumscribed relationships are a favorite SAT topic because they combine 3D geometry with algebraic reasoning.

### 6.5.4 Cylinder

A cylinder has two congruent circular bases and a curved lateral surface.

- **Volume**: $V = \pi r^2 h$ (area of base × height)
- **Surface Area**: $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$ (two circular bases + lateral surface area)
  - The lateral surface area $2\pi rh$ is the area of a rectangle with length equal to the circumference of the base ($2\pi r$) and width equal to the height ($h$).

**SAT note**: The SAT reference sheet provides the volume formula for a cylinder but **not** the surface area formula. If a surface area question appears, you must know $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$.

### 6.5.5 Sphere

A sphere is the set of all points in space equidistant from a fixed center point.

- **Volume**: $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$
- **Surface Area**: $SA = 4\pi r^2$

**Important relationship**: The surface area of a sphere is exactly 4 times the area of a great circle (a circle with the same radius as the sphere). This is a useful conceptual check.

**SAT note**: The volume formula is provided on the reference sheet, but the surface area formula is **not**. Memorize $SA = 4\pi r^2$.

### 6.5.6 Cone

A cone has a circular base and a single vertex (apex).

- **Volume**: $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ (one-third the volume of a cylinder with the same base and height)
- **Surface Area**: $SA = \pi r^2 + \pi rl$, where $l$ is the **slant height** (the distance from the apex to a point on the edge of the base, measured along the surface)
  - $\pi r^2$ is the area of the base.
  - $\pi rl$ is the lateral surface area.

**Slant height vs. vertical height**: The slant height $l$ is **not** the same as the vertical height $h$. They are related by the Pythagorean theorem: $l = \sqrt{r^2 + h^2}$. The SAT sometimes gives you one and asks for the other, or gives you both and asks for volume or surface area.

### 6.5.7 Pyramid

A pyramid has a polygonal base and triangular faces that meet at a single apex.

- **Volume**: $V = \frac{1}{3}Bh$, where $B$ is the area of the base and $h$ is the vertical height.
- For a square pyramid: $V = \frac{1}{3}s^2 h$

**SAT note**: The volume formula for a pyramid is provided on the reference sheet. Surface area formulas for pyramids are generally not tested.

### 6.5.8 Scaling and Proportional Reasoning in 3D

This is one of the most important and frequently tested concepts in SAT geometry. When a figure is scaled by a factor $k$:

- **Linear dimensions** (lengths, radii, heights, perimeters) scale by $k$.
- **Areas** (surface areas, cross-sectional areas) scale by $k^2$.
- **Volumes** scale by $k^3$.

**Why this matters**: The SAT loves to ask questions like "If the radius of a sphere is doubled, by what factor does the volume increase?" The answer is $2^3 = 8$, not 2 or 4.

**Common scaling scenarios:**

| Change | Linear | Area | Volume |
|---|---|---|---|
| Scale by 2 | $2\times$ | $4\times$ | $8\times$ |
| Scale by 3 | $3\times$ | $9\times$ | $27\times$ |
| Scale by 10 | $10\times$ | $100\times$ | $1000\times$ |
| Scale by $\frac{1}{2}$ | $\frac{1}{2}\times$ | $\frac{1}{4}\times$ | $\frac{1}{8}\times$ |
| Increase by 10% ($k = 1.10$) | $1.10\times$ | $1.21\times$ | $1.331\times$ |

**Percent change and scaling**: If a dimension increases by $p\%$, the scale factor is $1 + \frac{p}{100}$. The area changes by a factor of $\left(1 + \frac{p}{100}\right)^2$ and the volume by $\left(1 + \frac{p}{100}\right)^3$.

**Example**: If the side length of a cube increases by 20%, the new volume is $(1.20)^3 = 1.728$ times the original — a 72.8% increase. The new surface area is $(1.20)^2 = 1.44$ times the original — a 44% increase.

### 6.5.9 Inscribed and Circumscribed Solids

The SAT occasionally tests problems where one solid is inscribed in (inside) or circumscribed around (outside) another. The key is to identify the **linking dimension** — the measurement that connects the two shapes.

**Common configurations:**

1. **Sphere inscribed in a cube**: The diameter of the sphere equals the side of the cube. $d_{\text{sphere}} = s_{\text{cube}}$, so $r_{\text{sphere}} = \frac{s}{2}$.

2. **Cube inscribed in a sphere**: The space diagonal of the cube equals the diameter of the sphere. $d_{\text{cube}} = d_{\text{sphere}}$, so $s\sqrt{3} = 2r_{\text{sphere}}$, giving $s = \frac{2r}{\sqrt{3}}$.

3. **Cylinder inscribed in a sphere**: The relationship is more complex and involves the Pythagorean theorem in 3D. The diameter of the sphere equals the diagonal of the cylinder's "bounding box."

4. **Cone inscribed in a sphere**: The apex and the base circle of the cone both lie on the sphere's surface.

**Strategy for inscribed solid problems:**
1. Draw a cross-section through the center of both solids.
2. Identify the right triangle that relates the dimensions.
3. Apply the Pythagorean theorem.
4. Solve for the unknown.

### 6.5.10 3D Problem-Solving Strategies

1. **Always identify what is being asked** — volume, surface area, a specific length, or a ratio. Different goals require different formulas.
2. **Check whether the question provides a diagram drawn to scale**. If not, draw your own sketch.
3. **For scaling problems**, identify the scale factor first, then apply the correct power ($k$, $k^2$, or $k^3$).
4. **For inscribed/circumscribed problems**, find the linking dimension and set up an equation.
5. **For "empty space" problems** (e.g., a solid with a hole drilled through it), compute the volume of the original solid and subtract the volume of the removed portion.
6. **For "filling" problems** (e.g., how many small spheres fit in a large box), be careful — spheres do not pack perfectly. The SAT will either specify the packing arrangement or ask for a theoretical maximum based on volume division.

---

## 6.6 Integrated Geometry: When Shapes Meet Algebra

The hardest SAT geometry questions are those that combine geometric reasoning with algebraic techniques. These questions test whether you can translate geometric relationships into equations and solve them.

### 6.6.1 Geometry on the Coordinate Plane

When geometric figures appear on the coordinate plane, you have access to the full toolkit of coordinate geometry:

- **Distance formula**: $d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$
- **Midpoint formula**: $M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$
- **Slope formula**: $m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$

**Verifying properties with coordinates:**

| Property | Coordinate Test |
|---|---|
| Parallel lines | Equal slopes |
| Perpendicular lines | Slopes are negative reciprocals ($m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1$) |
| Congruent segments | Equal distances (distance formula) |
| Midpoint | Midpoint formula |
| Right angle | Perpendicular slopes at the vertex |

**Example**: To verify that a quadrilateral with vertices at $A(1, 2)$, $B(4, 6)$, $C(7, 4)$, and $D(4, 0)$ is a parallelogram, check that opposite sides have equal slopes:
- Slope of $AB = \frac{6-2}{4-1} = \frac{4}{3}$
- Slope of $CD = \frac{0-4}{4-7} = \frac{-4}{-3} = \frac{4}{3}$ ✓
- Slope of $BC = \frac{4-6}{7-4} = \frac{-2}{3}$
- Slope of $AD = \frac{0-2}{4-1} = \frac{-2}{3}$ ✓

### 6.6.2 Circles and the Coordinate Plane

The equation $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ is the bridge between circles and algebra. SAT questions in this area include:

1. **Finding the center and radius** from the equation (or vice versa).
2. **Determining whether a point lies inside, on, or outside a circle**: Substitute the point's coordinates into the left side of the equation. If the result equals $r^2$, the point is on the circle. If less than $r^2$, it is inside. If greater than $r^2$, it is outside.
3. **Finding the equation of a circle** given three points on the circle (substitute each point into the general form and solve the system).
4. **Finding intersection points** of a circle and a line (substitute the line equation into the circle equation and solve the resulting quadratic).
5. **Tangent lines to circles**: A line is tangent to a circle if the distance from the center to the line equals the radius.

### 6.6.3 Similar Figures and Proportional Reasoning

Similar figures appear throughout SAT geometry — in triangles, circles, polygons, and 3D solids. The core principle:

> If two figures are similar with scale factor $k$, then all corresponding lengths are in ratio $k$, all corresponding areas are in ratio $k^2$, and all corresponding volumes are in ratio $k^3$.

**In triangles specifically:**
- AA (Angle-Angle) similarity: If two angles of one triangle equal two angles of another, the triangles are similar. This is the most commonly used similarity criterion on the SAT.
- SSS similarity: All three side ratios are equal.
- SAS similarity: Two side ratios are equal and the included angles are congruent.

**Setting up proportions for similar triangles:**
1. Identify corresponding sides (match by angle size or position).
2. Write the proportion: $\frac{\text{side}_1 \text{ in } \Delta A}{\text{corresponding side}_1 \text{ in } \Delta B} = \frac{\text{side}_2 \text{ in } \Delta A}{\text{corresponding side}_2 \text{ in } \Delta B}$
3. Cross-multiply and solve.

### 6.6.4 Geometry and Quadratic Equations

Some of the most challenging SAT geometry problems involve setting up a quadratic equation from a geometric relationship. Common scenarios:

1. **Area problems with unknown dimensions**: "A rectangle has length 3 more than its width, and its area is 40. Find the dimensions." This gives $w(w + 3) = 40$, which expands to $w^2 + 3w - 40 = 0$.

2. **Right triangle problems**: "The hypotenuse of a right triangle is 13, and one leg is 7 more than the other." This gives $x^2 + (x + 7)^2 = 169$.

3. **Circle-line intersection**: Substituting a line equation into a circle equation produces a quadratic. The discriminant tells you whether the line intersects the circle (discriminant > 0), is tangent to it (discriminant = 0), or misses it entirely (discriminant < 0).

---

## 6.7 Comprehensive Formula Reference for This Chapter

### Formulas Provided on the SAT Reference Sheet

| Formula | Expression |
|---|---|
| Area of a circle | $A = \pi r^2$ |
| Circumference | $C = 2\pi r$ |
| Area of a rectangle | $A = lw$ |
| Area of a triangle | $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$ |
| Pythagorean theorem | $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ |
| Volume of rectangular solid | $V = lwh$ |
| Volume of cylinder | $V = \pi r^2 h$ |
| Volume of sphere | $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ |
| Volume of cone | $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ |
| Volume of pyramid | $V = \frac{1}{3}lwh$ |
| 360 degrees in a circle | — |
| 2$\pi$ radians in a circle | — |
| 180 degrees in a triangle | — |

### Formulas You Must Memorize

**Circles:**
- Arc length (degrees): $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times 2\pi r$
- Arc length (radians): $r\theta$
- Sector area (degrees): $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times \pi r^2$
- Sector area (radians): $\frac{1}{2}r^2\theta$
- Inscribed angle = $\frac{1}{2} \times$ intercepted arc
- Circle equation: $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$

**Polygons:**
- Sum of interior angles: $(n - 2) \times 180^\circ$
- Sum of exterior angles: $360^\circ$
- Each interior angle (regular $n$-gon): $\frac{(n-2) \times 180^\circ}{n}$
- Each exterior angle (regular $n$-gon): $\frac{360^\circ}{n}$

**Quadrilaterals:**
- Parallelogram area: $A = bh$
- Rhombus area (diagonals): $A = \frac{1}{2}d_1 d_2$
- Trapezoid area: $A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h$
- Sum of interior angles: $360^\circ$

**Solid Figures:**
- Surface area of cube: $SA = 6s^2$
- Surface area of rectangular solid: $SA = 2lw + 2lh + 2wh$
- Surface area of cylinder: $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$
- Surface area of sphere: $SA = 4\pi r^2$
- Surface area of cone: $SA = \pi r^2 + \pi rl$
- Diagonal of rectangular solid: $d = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2 + h^2}$
- Diagonal of cube: $d = s\sqrt{3}$

**Coordinate Geometry:**
- Distance: $d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$
- Midpoint: $M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$
- Slope: $m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$

---

## 6.8 Strategic Summary: How to Approach Any SAT Geometry Question

### Step 1: Classify the Question
Identify which geometric domain the question belongs to: circles, triangles, quadrilaterals, polygons, solids, or coordinate geometry. This determines which formulas and theorems are relevant.

### Step 2: Extract All Given Information
Write down every measurement, relationship, and constraint stated in the problem. If a diagram is provided, label it with all given values. If no diagram is provided, draw one.

### Step 3: Identify What Is Being Asked
Be specific. Are you solving for a length, an angle, an area, a volume, a coordinate, or a ratio? The target determines your strategy.

### Step 4: Select the Appropriate Tool(s)
Choose the formula, theorem, or algebraic technique that connects the given information to the unknown. This may involve:
- A direct formula application
- Setting up a proportion (similar figures, ratios)
- Writing and solving an equation (Pythagorean theorem, circle equation, area formula)
- Using angle relationships (supplementary, complementary, vertical, inscribed)
- Applying scaling principles ($k$, $k^2$, $k^3$)

### Step 5: Execute and Verify
Carry out the calculation. Check that your answer:
- Has the correct units (length vs. area vs. volume)
- Is reasonable in magnitude (does a radius of 500 make sense for a circle inscribed in a square of side 10?)
- Answers the specific question asked (not $x$ when the question asks for $2x$)

### Step 6: Use Desmos When Advantageous
For circle equations, systems involving geometry and algebra, and "how many solutions" questions, Desmos can provide rapid visual confirmation. Graph the circle, click on intersection points, and read exact coordinates.

---

## 6.9 Common SAT Geometry Traps and How to Avoid Them

### Trap 1: Confusing Arc Length with Sector Area
Arc length is a linear measure; sector area is a square measure. They use similar proportional formulas but produce different types of quantities. Always check what the question is asking for.

### Trap 2: Using the Slant Height Instead of the Perpendicular Height
In parallelograms, trapezoids, and triangles, the height must be **perpendicular** to the base. The SAT sometimes gives you the slanted side length and expects you to either recognize it is not the height or use the Pythagorean theorem to find the actual height.

### Trap 3: Forgetting That Area Scales by $k^2$ and Volume by $k^3$
When a dimension changes by a percentage, students often apply the same percentage to area and volume. Remember: linear dimensions scale by $k$, areas by $k^2$, volumes by $k^3$.

### Trap 4: Misidentifying the Center from a Circle Equation
The equation $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ has center $(h, k)$, not $(-h, -k)$. The signs are counterintuitive. Practice identifying centers quickly.

### Trap 5: Assuming a Figure Is Drawn to Scale
Unless the problem states "figure is drawn to scale" or you can confirm it, do not trust visual judgments about angle measures or side lengths. Rely on given information and mathematical reasoning.

### Trap 6: Confusing Surface Area and Volume
These are fundamentally different quantities. Surface area is measured in square units; volume in cubic units. The SAT includes answer choices that represent the wrong quantity — students who compute volume when surface area is asked (or vice versa) will find a wrong answer that matches one of the choices.

### Trap 7: Forgetting the $\frac{1}{3}$ Factor for Cones and Pyramids
The volume of a cone or pyramid is $\frac{1}{3}$ the volume of a cylinder or prism with the same base and height. Students who forget the $\frac{1}{3}$ will compute a volume that is too large by a factor of 3.

### Trap 8: Not Checking Calculator Mode (Degrees vs. Radians)
If a problem involves radians and your calculator is in degree mode (or vice versa), your trig values will be wrong. Always verify the mode before computing trig functions.

---

## 6.10 Connections to Other Chapters

The geometry in this chapter does not exist in isolation. Here is how it connects to other major topics:

- **Chapter I (Linear Functions)**: The slope and equation of a line are essential for coordinate geometry problems involving parallel and perpendicular lines, which frequently appear in quadrilateral and circle problems.
- **Chapter II (Systems of Equations)**: Finding the intersection of a line and a circle requires solving a system of one linear and one quadratic equation.
- **Chapter III (Quadratics)**: The equation of a circle is a quadratic in two variables. Completing the square converts between forms. The discriminant determines whether a line intersects a circle.
- **Chapter V (Functions)**: The circle equation does not represent a function (it fails the vertical line test), but semicircles do. Domain and range questions involving circles test this understanding.
- **Chapter VIII (Ratios and Proportions)**: Similar triangles, scale factors, and proportional reasoning are the backbone of many geometry problems.
- **Chapter XI (Trigonometry)**: Right triangle trigonometry is used to find missing sides and angles in geometric figures, especially when combined with circles (inscribed angles, tangent lines).
- **Chapter XV (Coordinate Geometry)**: The distance formula, midpoint formula, and slope formula are the algebraic tools that power coordinate geometry.

Mastering this chapter means not only memorizing formulas but understanding the web of relationships that connect geometry to algebra, functions, and data analysis. The SAT rewards this integrated understanding.

---

*This chapter has provided a comprehensive, deep-dive treatment of circles, polygons, quadrilaterals, and solid figures as they appear on the Digital SAT. The formulas, strategies, and trap-avoidance techniques presented here form the foundation for tackling every geometry question the test can produce. Continue to the next chapters for the remaining geometry domains: coordinate geometry, trigonometry, and data analysis.*

# Chapter 7: Trigonometry, Coordinate Geometry, and Functions

---

## Part I: Trigonometry — The Language of Right Triangles

### 1.1 What Is Trigonometry?

Trigonometry is the branch of mathematics that studies the relationships between the side lengths and the angles of triangles. On the SAT, trigonometry is confined almost entirely to **right triangles** and the **unit circle**. You will not encounter oblique (non-right) triangle trigonometry, nor will you need the Law of Sines or Law of Cosines. The SAT tests trigonometry in a focused, predictable way: you must be fluent in the definitions of sine, cosine, and tangent; you must understand the complementary angle identity; and you must be able to convert between degrees and radians and interpret the equation of a circle.

The word "trigonometry" comes from the Greek words *trigonon* (triangle) and *metron* (measure). At its core, trigonometry answers a simple question: **If I know some angles and some side lengths of a right triangle, can I determine the remaining angles and side lengths?** The answer is yes — and the tools that make this possible are the six trigonometric ratios.

### 1.2 The Six Trigonometric Functions

Consider a right triangle with acute angles *A* and *B* and a right angle *C*. Label the sides relative to angle *A*:

- **Opposite side (O):** the side directly across from angle *A*
- **Adjacent side (A):** the side that forms angle *A* together with the hypotenuse (i.e., the leg that touches angle *A* but is not the hypotenuse)
- **Hypotenuse (H):** the longest side, always opposite the right angle

The six trigonometric functions of angle *A* are defined as ratios of these three sides:

| Function | Abbreviation | Ratio | Mnemonic |
|---|---|---|---|
| Sine | sin(A) | Opposite / Hypotenuse | **SOH** |
| Cosine | cos(A) | Adjacent / Hypotenuse | **CAH** |
| Tangent | tan(A) | Opposite / Adjacent | **TOA** |
| Cosecant | csc(A) | Hypotenuse / Opposite | Reciprocal of sine |
| Secant | sec(A) | Hypotenuse / Adjacent | Reciprocal of cosine |
| Cotangent | cot(A) | Adjacent / Opposite | Reciprocal of tangent |

**SOH CAH TOA** is the foundational mnemonic. Every trigonometric calculation on the SAT begins with correctly identifying which sides are opposite, adjacent, and hypotenuse relative to the angle you are working with.

**Critical detail:** The labels "opposite" and "adjacent" are **relative to the angle you are analyzing**. If you switch from angle *A* to angle *B*, the opposite and adjacent sides swap. The hypotenuse never changes — it is always the side opposite the right angle.

#### The Reciprocal Functions

The three reciprocal functions — cosecant, secant, and cotangent — are simply the multiplicative inverses of sine, cosine, and tangent:

$$\csc(A) = \frac{1}{\sin(A)} = \frac{\text{Hypotenuse}}{\text{Opposite}}$$

$$\sec(A) = \frac{1}{\cos(A)} = \frac{\text{Hypotenuse}}{\text{Adjacent}}$$

$$\cot(A) = \frac{1}{\tan(A)} = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Opposite}}$$

On the SAT, you will primarily use sine, cosine, and tangent. The reciprocal functions appear rarely, but you should recognize them and know how to compute them if needed.

### 1.3 Using Trigonometry to Solve for Missing Sides and Angles

Trigonometry serves two fundamental purposes in right triangles:

**Purpose 1: Finding a missing side length.**
- You need to know **one acute angle** (besides the right angle) and **one side length**.
- Set up the appropriate trig ratio (sin, cos, or tan) using the known angle, the known side, and the unknown side.
- Solve the resulting equation.

**Purpose 2: Finding a missing angle.**
- You need to know **at least two side lengths**.
- Set up the appropriate trig ratio using the two known sides.
- Use the **inverse trig functions** (sin⁻¹, cos⁻¹, tan⁻¹) to find the angle.

#### Step-by-Step Procedure for Any Trig Problem

1. **Choose the acute angle** you will work with (the one given, or the one opposite a given side).
2. **Label the three sides** relative to that angle: Opposite, Adjacent, Hypotenuse.
3. **Identify which two sides** are involved in the problem (one may be unknown).
4. **Select the trig function** that relates those two sides:
   - Opposite + Hypotenuse → use **sine**
   - Adjacent + Hypotenuse → use **cosine**
   - Opposite + Adjacent → use **tangent**
5. **Set up the equation** with the known values and the variable.
6. **Solve** using algebra. If the variable is in the denominator, multiply both sides by it first. If you are solving for an angle, apply the inverse trig function.

#### Worked Conceptual Example

Imagine a right triangle where the hypotenuse has length 10, and one acute angle measures 30°. To find the side opposite the 30° angle:

- Relative to the 30° angle: the unknown side is the **Opposite**, and the known side (10) is the **Hypotenuse**.
- The function linking Opposite and Hypotenuse is **sine**.
- Set up: $\sin(30°) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{10}$
- Since $\sin(30°) = \frac{1}{2}$: $\frac{1}{2} = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{10}$
- Multiply both sides by 10: Opposite = 5

This is consistent with the 30-60-90 special right triangle: the side opposite 30° is always half the hypotenuse.

### 1.4 The Complementary Angle Identity (Cofunction Identity)

One of the most frequently tested trigonometric concepts on the SAT is the relationship between the sine and cosine of complementary angles.

**Definition:** Two angles are complementary if their measures sum to 90°.

**The Identity:**

$$\sin(x°) = \cos(90° - x°)$$

$$\cos(x°) = \sin(90° - x°)$$

**Why does this work?** In a right triangle, the two acute angles always sum to 90° (since the total is 180° and the right angle accounts for 90°). The side that is **opposite** one acute angle is **adjacent** to the other acute angle. Therefore:

- $\sin(A) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$ relative to angle $A$
- $\cos(B) = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$ relative to angle $B$

But the side opposite angle $A$ is the same side that is adjacent to angle $B$. And the hypotenuse is shared. Therefore $\sin(A) = \cos(B)$ when $A + B = 90°$.

**SAT Application:** If a question gives you $\sin(37°)$ and asks for $\cos(53°)$, you can immediately conclude they are equal because 37° + 53° = 90°. No calculator needed.

**Extended understanding:** This identity also tells us that in any right triangle:

$$\sin(A) = \cos(B) \quad \text{and} \quad \cos(A) = \sin(B)$$

where $A$ and $B$ are the two acute angles.

### 1.5 The Pythagorean Identity

The Pythagorean identity connects sine and cosine of the same angle:

$$\sin^2(\theta) + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$$

This is simply the Pythagorean theorem applied to the unit circle (a circle of radius 1 centered at the origin). If a point on the unit circle has coordinates $(\cos\theta, \sin\theta)$, then by the distance formula from the origin:

$$x^2 + y^2 = 1 \implies \cos^2(\theta) + \sin^2(\theta) = 1$$

**SAT Application:** If you are given $\sin(\theta) = \frac{3}{5}$ and told that $\theta$ is acute, you can find $\cos(\theta)$:

$$\sin^2(\theta) + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$$

$$\left(\frac{3}{5}\right)^2 + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$$

$$\frac{9}{25} + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$$

$$\cos^2(\theta) = \frac{16}{25}$$

$$\cos(\theta) = \frac{4}{5}$$

(We take the positive root because $\theta$ is acute, meaning it lies in the first quadrant where cosine is positive.)

### 1.6 Degrees and Radians

Angles can be measured in two units: **degrees** and **radians**. The SAT tests your ability to convert between them and to work comfortably in either system.

**The fundamental relationship:**

$$180° = \pi \text{ radians}$$

**Conversion formulas:**

- **Degrees → Radians:** multiply by $\frac{\pi}{180}$
- **Radians → Degrees:** multiply by $\frac{180}{\pi}$

#### Common Angle Conversions

| Degrees | Radians |
|---|---|
| 30° | $\frac{\pi}{6}$ |
| 45° | $\frac{\pi}{4}$ |
| 60° | $\frac{\pi}{3}$ |
| 90° | $\frac{\pi}{2}$ |
| 120° | $\frac{2\pi}{3}$ |
| 135° | $\frac{3\pi}{4}$ |
| 150° | $\frac{5\pi}{6}$ |
| 180° | $\pi$ |
| 270° | $\frac{3\pi}{2}$ |
| 360° | $2\pi$ |

**How to derive any conversion:** Set up a proportion. If you want to convert 150° to radians:

$$\frac{150°}{180°} = \frac{x}{\pi}$$

$$x = \frac{150\pi}{180} = \frac{5\pi}{6}$$

**SAT Tip:** The Desmos calculator defaults to **degrees**. If a problem involves radians, you must switch Desmos to radian mode (click the wrench icon in the top-right corner of the graphing area and select "radians"). Forgetting to switch modes is one of the most common calculator errors on the SAT.

### 1.7 The Unit Circle

The unit circle is a circle of radius 1 centered at the origin (0, 0) of the coordinate plane. It is the most important visual tool in trigonometry because it extends the definitions of sine and cosine to all angles (not just acute angles in right triangles).

**Key principle:** For any angle $\theta$ measured from the positive x-axis, the point where the terminal side of the angle intersects the unit circle has coordinates:

$$(\cos\theta, \sin\theta)$$

This means:
- The **x-coordinate** of the point on the unit circle equals **$\cos(\theta)$**
- The **y-coordinate** of the point on the unit circle equals **$\sin(\theta)$**

#### Quadrant Sign Patterns

The sign of sine and cosine depends on which quadrant the terminal side of the angle lies in:

| Quadrant | x-coordinate (cos) | y-coordinate (sin) | Mnemonic |
|---|---|---|---|
| I (0° to 90°) | Positive | Positive | **A**ll positive |
| II (90° to 180°) | Negative | Positive | **S**ine positive |
| III (180° to 270°) | Negative | Negative | **T**angent positive |
| IV (270° to 360°) | Positive | Negative | **C**osine positive |

The mnemonic **ASTC** ("All Students Take Calculus") helps you remember which trig functions are positive in each quadrant.

#### Reference Angles

For any angle in standard position, its **reference angle** is the acute angle it makes with the x-axis. The sine and cosine of any angle have the same absolute value as the sine and cosine of its reference angle — only the sign changes based on the quadrant.

**Example:** The reference angle for 150° is 30° (since 150° = 180° − 30°). Therefore:
- $|\sin(150°)| = \sin(30°) = \frac{1}{2}$, and since 150° is in Quadrant II where sine is positive: $\sin(150°) = \frac{1}{2}$
- $|\cos(150°)| = \cos(30°) = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$, and since 150° is in Quadrant II where cosine is negative: $\cos(150°) = -\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$

### 1.8 Trigonometry with Special Right Triangles

The 30-60-90 and 45-45-90 triangles are the backbone of exact trigonometric values. You should know the sine, cosine, and tangent of 30°, 45°, and 60° from memory.

#### 45°-45°-90° Triangle (sides: 1, 1, $\sqrt{2}$)

| Function | Value |
|---|---|
| $\sin(45°)$ | $\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$ |
| $\cos(45°)$ | $\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$ |
| $\tan(45°)$ | 1 |

#### 30°-60°-90° Triangle (sides: 1, $\sqrt{3}$, 2)

| Function | Value |
|---|---|
| $\sin(30°)$ | $\frac{1}{2}$ |
| $\cos(30°)$ | $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ |
| $\tan(30°)$ | $\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{3}$ |
| $\sin(60°)$ | $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ |
| $\cos(60°)$ | $\frac{1}{2}$ |
| $\tan(60°)$ | $\sqrt{3}$ |

**Memory tip:** For 30° and 60°, sine always involves the "smaller" number ($\frac{1}{2}$ or $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$) and cosine is the other one. Tangent is $\frac{\text{opposite}}{\text{adjacent}}$, so $\tan(30°) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}$ (small over large) and $\tan(60°) = \sqrt{3}$ (large over small).

### 1.9 Trigonometry on the SAT — What to Expect

The SAT typically includes **2–3 trigonometry questions** per test. These questions fall into several categories:

1. **Basic ratio application:** Given a right triangle with some sides and angles, find a missing side or angle using sin, cos, or tan.

2. **Complementary angle identity:** Recognizing that $\sin(x) = \cos(90° − x)$ and using this to solve problems without a calculator.

3. **Pythagorean identity:** Given $\sin(\theta)$ or $\cos(\theta)$, find the other using $\sin^2\theta + \cos^2\theta = 1$.

4. **Radians and degrees:** Converting between the two units, or working with arc length/sector area formulas in radians.

5. **Circle equation:** Recognizing that $(x − h)^2 + (y − k)^2 = r^2$ describes a circle with center $(h, k)$ and radius $r$, and connecting this to the unit circle concept.

6. **Equivalent expressions:** Being asked which expression is equivalent to a given trig expression (often testing the complementary angle identity or Pythagorean identity in algebraic form).

**Key strategy:** If a trig question gives you a right triangle diagram, immediately label all sides relative to the given angle. If no diagram is provided, draw one. The visual representation is essential for avoiding errors in identifying opposite, adjacent, and hypotenuse.

---

## Part II: Coordinate Geometry — Algebra Meets Geometry

### 2.1 The Coordinate Plane

The coordinate plane (also called the Cartesian plane) is a two-dimensional surface defined by two perpendicular number lines:

- The **x-axis** runs horizontally (left-right).
- The **y-axis** runs vertically (up-down).
- The **origin** is the point (0, 0) where the axes intersect.

Every point on the plane is identified by an **ordered pair** $(x, y)$, where:
- $x$ is the horizontal displacement from the origin (positive = right, negative = left)
- $y$ is the vertical displacement from the origin (positive = up, negative = down)

The axes divide the plane into **four quadrants**, numbered counterclockwise starting from the upper right:

| Quadrant | x-sign | y-sign | Example |
|---|---|---|---|
| I | + | + | (3, 5) |
| II | − | + | (−2, 4) |
| III | − | − | (−1, −6) |
| IV | + | − | (7, −3) |

Points on the axes (where $x = 0$ or $y = 0$) are not in any quadrant.

### 2.2 The Distance Formula

The distance between two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ is derived from the Pythagorean theorem:

$$d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$$

**Derivation:** Imagine the two points form the hypotenuse of a right triangle. The horizontal leg has length $|x_2 - x_1|$ and the vertical leg has length $|y_2 - y_1|$. By the Pythagorean theorem:

$$d^2 = (x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2$$

$$d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$$

**SAT Application:** The distance formula is used to find lengths of segments, radii of circles, and to verify properties of geometric figures placed on the coordinate plane. It is **not provided** on the SAT reference sheet — you must memorize it.

**Common error to avoid:** Do not mix up the x and y coordinates. The formula is symmetric, so $(x_2 - x_1)^2 = (x_1 - x_2)^2$, but you must be consistent within a single calculation.

### 2.3 The Midpoint Formula

The midpoint of the segment connecting $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ is:

$$M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$$

**Conceptual understanding:** The midpoint is simply the **average** of the x-coordinates and the **average** of the y-coordinates. This makes intuitive sense — the point halfway between two values is their arithmetic mean.

**Derivation:** If you travel from $(x_1, y_1)$ to $(x_2, y_2)$, the total horizontal change is $(x_2 - x_1)$ and the total vertical change is $(y_2 - y_1)$. To reach the midpoint, you travel half of each change:

$$x_{\text{mid}} = x_1 + \frac{x_2 - x_1}{2} = \frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}$$

$$y_{\text{mid}} = y_1 + \frac{y_2 - y_1}{2} = \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}$$

**SAT Application:** The midpoint formula appears in problems involving:
- Finding the center of a circle given the endpoints of a diameter
- Finding the intersection of diagonals in a parallelogram (diagonals bisect each other)
- Finding the midpoint of a segment in geometric proofs on the coordinate plane

### 2.4 Slope — The Measure of Steepness

The slope of a line measures its steepness and direction. It is defined as the ratio of the vertical change (rise) to the horizontal change (run) between any two points on the line:

$$m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1} = \frac{\text{rise}}{\text{run}} = \frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}$$

**Interpreting slope values:**

| Slope Value | Meaning |
|---|---|
| $m > 0$ | Line rises from left to right (increasing) |
| $m < 0$ | Line falls from left to right (decreasing) |
| $m = 0$ | Horizontal line (no vertical change) |
| $m$ undefined | Vertical line (no horizontal change, division by zero) |
| $|m| > 1$ | Steep line (rises more than it runs) |
| $|m| < 1$ | Shallow line (runs more than it rises) |
| $m = 1$ | 45° angle upward |
| $m = -1$ | 45° angle downward |

**Critical property:** Slope is **constant** along a straight line. No matter which two points you choose, you will always get the same slope value. This is what makes a line "straight" — the rate of change never varies.

**SAT Application:** Slope is one of the most frequently tested concepts on the SAT. You must be able to:
- Calculate slope from two points
- Interpret slope in context (as a rate of change)
- Determine if lines are parallel (same slope) or perpendicular (negative reciprocal slopes)
- Identify the slope from a linear equation in any form

### 2.5 Equations of Lines — All Forms

A line on the coordinate plane can be described by an equation. The SAT tests three forms, each with distinct advantages:

#### Slope-Intercept Form: $y = mx + b$

- **$m$** = slope
- **$b$** = y-intercept (the y-coordinate where the line crosses the y-axis)
- **Best for:** Quickly identifying slope and y-intercept; graphing a line
- **How to graph:** Plot the y-intercept $(0, b)$, then use the slope to find another point (rise over run from the intercept)

#### Point-Slope Form: $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$

- **$m$** = slope
- **$(x_1, y_1)$** = any point on the line
- **Best for:** Writing the equation of a line when you know one point and the slope
- **Note:** You can use **any** point on the line for $(x_1, y_1)$; the resulting equations are all equivalent

#### Standard Form: $Ax + By = C$

- **$A, B, C$** are integers (A should be positive by convention)
- **Best for:** Systems of equations; certain word problems
- **Slope from standard form:** $m = -\frac{A}{B}$
- **y-intercept from standard form:** $b = \frac{C}{B}$

#### Converting Between Forms

**Point-slope → Slope-intercept:** Distribute the slope, then add $y_1$ to both sides to isolate $y$.

**Point-slope → Standard:** Distribute, then move all variable terms to one side and the constant to the other. Multiply through by the LCD if fractions are present.

**Standard → Slope-intercept:** Subtract $Ax$ from both sides, then divide by $B$.

**Slope-intercept → Standard:** Subtract $mx$ from both sides (or move the x term), then multiply through to clear fractions and make $A$ positive.

### 2.6 Parallel and Perpendicular Lines

#### Parallel Lines

Two lines are **parallel** if and only if they have the **same slope** and different y-intercepts.

- If line 1 has slope $m_1$ and line 2 has slope $m_2$, then the lines are parallel when $m_1 = m_2$.
- If the lines also have the same y-intercept, they are the **same line** (coincident), not merely parallel.

**SAT Application:** Questions often give you two lines in different forms and ask whether they are parallel. Convert both to slope-intercept form and compare slopes.

#### Perpendicular Lines

Two lines are **perpendicular** if and only if their slopes are **negative reciprocals** of each other.

- If line 1 has slope $m_1$ and line 2 has slope $m_2$, then the lines are perpendicular when $m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1$.
- Equivalently: $m_2 = -\frac{1}{m_1}$

**Examples:**
- A line with slope 3 is perpendicular to a line with slope −1/3.
- A line with slope −2/5 is perpendicular to a line with slope 5/2.
- A horizontal line (slope 0) is perpendicular to a vertical line (undefined slope).

**How to find the negative reciprocal:**
1. Flip the fraction (take the reciprocal)
2. Change the sign

**SAT Application:** Perpendicular slope problems appear frequently. A common question type gives you the equation of a line and asks which of several lines is perpendicular to it. Find the slope of the given line, compute its negative reciprocal, and check which answer choice has that slope.

### 2.7 The Equation of a Circle

The standard form equation of a circle is:

$$(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

where:
- **$(h, k)$** is the center of the circle
- **$r$** is the radius

**Understanding the equation:** This is simply the distance formula in disguise. It says: "the set of all points $(x, y)$ whose distance from the center $(h, k)$ equals $r$."

**Key details:**
- The x-coordinate of the center is **$h$**, but it appears as **$(x - h)$** in the equation. So if you see $(x - 3)^2$, the x-coordinate of the center is +3. If you see $(x + 5)^2$, rewrite as $(x - (-5))^2$, so the center's x-coordinate is −5.
- The right side is **$r^2$**, not $r$. If the equation equals 25, the radius is 5, not 25.

#### Expanded Form → Standard Form (Completing the Square)

Sometimes a circle equation is given in expanded form:

$$x^2 + y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0$$

To convert to standard form, **complete the square** for the x-terms and the y-terms separately:

1. Group x-terms together and y-terms together.
2. For the x-group: take half the coefficient of x, square it, and add to both sides.
3. For the y-group: take half the coefficient of y, square it, and add to both sides.
4. Factor each group as a perfect square trinomial.
5. The equation is now in standard form; read off the center and radius.

**SAT Application:** Circle equations appear on the SAT in several ways:
- Given the equation, identify the center or radius
- Given the center and radius, write the equation
- Given the expanded form, complete the square to find the center or radius
- Determine if a point lies inside, on, or outside a circle (substitute the point; if the left side equals $r^2$, it's on the circle; if less, inside; if more, outside)

### 2.8 Reflections and Symmetry

Reflecting a point across an axis or the origin changes the sign of one or both coordinates:

| Reflection | Rule | Example |
|---|---|---|
| Across x-axis | $(x, y) \to (x, -y)$ | $(3, 5) \to (3, -5)$ |
| Across y-axis | $(x, y) \to (-x, y)$ | $(3, 5) \to (-3, 5)$ |
| Across origin | $(x, y) \to (-x, -y)$ | $(3, 5) \to (-3, -5)$ |

**Conceptual understanding:**
- Reflecting across the x-axis flips the point vertically (the y-coordinate changes sign).
- Reflecting across the y-axis flips the point horizontally (the x-coordinate changes sign).
- Reflecting across the origin is equivalent to reflecting across both axes (both coordinates change sign).

**SAT Application:** Reflection problems may ask you to find the image of a point after a reflection, or to determine the line of reflection given a point and its image. They may also ask about the symmetry of graphs (e.g., "If the graph of a function is symmetric about the y-axis, which equation could represent it?").

### 2.9 Coordinate Geometry and Geometric Figures

The SAT frequently places geometric figures on the coordinate plane and asks you to analyze their properties. Common scenarios include:

**Triangles on the coordinate plane:**
- Finding the area using the base and height (often one side is horizontal or vertical, making the height easy to compute)
- Verifying that a triangle is right (check if any two sides have slopes that are negative reciprocals)
- Finding the length of a side using the distance formula

**Quadrilaterals on the coordinate plane:**
- Verifying that a quadrilateral is a parallelogram (opposite sides have the same slope)
- Verifying that a quadrilateral is a rectangle (adjacent sides have slopes that are negative reciprocals)
- Verifying that diagonals bisect each other (midpoint of one diagonal equals midpoint of the other)

**Circles on the coordinate plane:**
- Using the standard form equation to find center and radius
- Determining if a point lies on a circle
- Finding the equation of a circle given geometric constraints

**Strategy:** When a geometry problem is placed on the coordinate plane, always write down the coordinates of all given points. Then apply the appropriate formulas (distance, slope, midpoint) systematically. Drawing a sketch, even a rough one, helps enormously.

### 2.10 Using Desmos for Coordinate Geometry

The built-in Desmos graphing calculator is an extraordinarily powerful tool for coordinate geometry problems:

- **Graphing lines:** Type any linear equation in any form. Desmos will graph it instantly.
- **Finding intersections:** Click on the intersection point of two lines to see the exact coordinates.
- **Graphing circles:** Type the circle equation in standard form. Desmos will display the circle.
- **Finding distance:** Plot two points, draw a segment between them, and Desmos shows the length.
- **Verifying properties:** Graph multiple objects and visually confirm relationships (parallel, perpendicular, tangent).

**Pro tip:** For "which of the following equations represents..." questions, graph each answer choice in Desmos and see which one matches the given graph or conditions. This is often faster than algebraic manipulation.

---

## Part III: Functions — The Machinery of Mathematics

### 3.1 What Is a Function?

A **function** is a rule that assigns to each input exactly one output. Think of it as a machine: you put something in (the input), the machine does something to it, and something comes out (the output).

**Formal definition:** A function $f$ is a set of ordered pairs $(x, y)$ such that no two ordered pairs have the same x-value but different y-values. In other words, each input maps to exactly one output.

**Function notation:** $f(x)$ represents the output of function $f$ when the input is $x$. It is read "f of x."

**Critical distinction:** $f(x)$ does NOT mean $f$ times $x$. It is notation that means "the value of the function $f$ at $x$." For example, if $f(x) = 2x + 3$, then $f(5) = 2(5) + 3 = 13$.

**The vertical line test:** A graph represents a function if and only if no vertical line intersects the graph more than once. If a vertical line would hit the graph at two or more points, then a single x-value would have multiple y-values, violating the definition of a function.

### 3.2 Evaluating Functions

To evaluate a function at a specific input, substitute that input for every instance of the variable in the function's rule.

**Example:** If $f(x) = x^2 - 4x + 7$, then:
- $f(3) = (3)^2 - 4(3) + 7 = 9 - 12 + 7 = 4$
- $f(-2) = (-2)^2 - 4(-2) + 7 = 4 + 8 + 7 = 19$
- $f(a) = a^2 - 4a + 7$ (substitute $a$ for $x$)
- $f(x + h) = (x + h)^2 - 4(x + h) + 7$ (substitute $x + h$ for $x$, then expand)

**SAT Application:** Function evaluation questions are straightforward but test your algebraic substitution skills. Common variations include:
- Evaluating at a number
- Evaluating at an expression (like $f(x + 3)$)
- Setting two function evaluations equal and solving for the variable
- Evaluating a function at the output of another function (composition)

### 3.3 Domain and Range

**Domain:** The set of all possible input values (x-values) for which the function is defined.

**Range:** The set of all possible output values (y-values) that the function can produce.

#### Finding the Domain

For most SAT problems, the domain restrictions come from:

1. **Denominators cannot be zero:** If $f(x) = \frac{1}{x - 3}$, then $x \neq 3$. The domain is all real numbers except 3.

2. **Expressions under square roots must be nonnegative:** If $f(x) = \sqrt{x + 5}$, then $x + 5 \geq 0$, so $x \geq -5$. The domain is $[-5, \infty)$.

3. **Logarithms (rare on SAT):** The argument must be positive.

**If no restrictions are present** (e.g., $f(x) = 2x + 3$), the domain is all real numbers.

#### Finding the Range

The range is often harder to determine than the domain. Methods include:

1. **Graph the function:** The range is the set of y-values the graph covers.
2. **Analyze the function type:**
   - Linear function (non-constant): range is all real numbers
   - Quadratic opening up: range is $[k, \infty)$ where $k$ is the minimum y-value (vertex)
   - Quadratic opening down: range is $(-\infty, k]$ where $k$ is the maximum y-value (vertex)
   - Absolute value: range is $[0, \infty)$ or $[k, \infty)$ depending on vertical shift

**SAT Application:** Domain and range questions on the SAT typically involve:
- Identifying the domain from a function equation (look for denominators and square roots)
- Identifying the range from a graph
- Determining a restricted domain from a real-world context (e.g., "the number of items must be a positive integer")

### 3.4 Function Composition

Function composition means applying one function to the output of another. The notation $f(g(x))$ means "first apply $g$ to $x$, then apply $f$ to the result." It is read "f of g of x."

**Step-by-step process:**
1. Start with the innermost function: evaluate $g(x)$.
2. Take that result and use it as the input for $f$.

**Example:** If $f(x) = x^2 + 1$ and $g(x) = 3x - 2$:
- $f(g(x)) = f(3x - 2) = (3x - 2)^2 + 1 = 9x^2 - 12x + 4 + 1 = 9x^2 - 12x + 5$
- $g(f(x)) = g(x^2 + 1) = 3(x^2 + 1) - 2 = 3x^2 + 3 - 2 = 3x^2 + 1$

**Important:** $f(g(x)) \neq g(f(x))$ in general. Composition is **not commutative**. The order matters.

**SAT Application:** Composition questions test your ability to substitute carefully. Common formats include:
- "If $f(x) = ...$ and $g(x) = ...$, what is $f(g(2))$?" — evaluate $g(2)$ first, then plug into $f$.
- "Which expression is equivalent to $f(g(x))$?" — compose and simplify.
- "If $f(g(x)) = ...$, and $f(x) = ...$, what is $g(x)$?" — work backwards.

### 3.5 "Wacky Functions" — Made-Up Notation

The SAT occasionally introduces a **new symbol** and defines a rule for it, then asks you to evaluate or manipulate it. These are sometimes called "wacky functions" or "defined operations."

**Example:** Let $a \star b = 2a + b^2$. What is the value of $3 \star 4$?

**Solution:** Substitute $a = 3$ and $b = 4$ into the rule:
$3 \star 4 = 2(3) + (4)^2 = 6 + 16 = 22$

**The key insight:** These problems are testing whether you can **follow instructions**. The symbol ($\star$, $\triangle$, $\square$, @, etc.) is arbitrary. What matters is the definition given in the problem. Read it carefully, substitute carefully, and compute carefully.

**Common variations:**
- Binary operations: $a \star b =$ some expression involving $a$ and $b$
- Unary operations: $\S x =$ some expression involving $x$
- Nested operations: evaluate from the innermost outward, just like composition

**Strategy:** Treat the made-up symbol exactly like function notation. If $a \star b = 2a + b^2$, think of it as a function of two variables: $f(a, b) = 2a + b^2$. The same principles of substitution apply.

### 3.6 Function Transformations

Understanding how changes to a function's equation affect its graph is essential for the SAT. Given a parent function $y = f(x)$, the following transformations apply:

#### Vertical Shifts
- **$y = f(x) + k$:** Shifts the graph **up** by $k$ units (if $k > 0$)
- **$y = f(x) - k$:** Shifts the graph **down** by $k$ units (if $k > 0$)

#### Horizontal Shifts
- **$y = f(x - h)$:** Shifts the graph **right** by $h$ units (if $h > 0$)
- **$y = f(x + h)$:** Shifts the graph **left** by $h$ units (if $h > 0$)

**Memory aid:** Horizontal shifts go the "opposite direction" of what you might expect. $f(x - 3)$ moves RIGHT, not LEFT. Think of it as: "What value of $x$ makes the inside zero? If it's $x - 3$, then $x = 3$, so the graph has shifted to the right."

#### Reflections
- **$y = -f(x)$:** Reflects the graph across the **x-axis** (flips it vertically)
- **$y = f(-x)$:** Reflects the graph across the **y-axis** (flips it horizontally)

#### Vertical Stretches and Compressions
- **$y = a \cdot f(x)$** where $|a| > 1$: **Stretches** the graph vertically (makes it taller and narrower)
- **$y = a \cdot f(x)$** where $0 < |a| < 1$: **Compresses** the graph vertically (makes it shorter and wider)

#### Horizontal Stretches and Compressions
- **$y = f(bx)$** where $|b| > 1$: **Compresses** the graph horizontally (makes it narrower)
- **$y = f(bx)$** where $0 < |b| < 1$: **Stretches** the graph horizontally (makes it wider)

**Note:** Horizontal stretches/compressions go the "opposite direction" of the coefficient, similar to horizontal shifts.

#### Combining Transformations

When multiple transformations are applied, the order matters. The standard order is:

1. Horizontal shift
2. Horizontal stretch/compression and reflection across y-axis
3. Vertical stretch/compression and reflection across x-axis
4. Vertical shift

**Example:** $y = -2f(x - 3) + 1$ means:
1. Shift $f(x)$ right by 3 units → $f(x - 3)$
2. Stretch vertically by a factor of 2 → $2f(x - 3)$
3. Reflect across the x-axis → $-2f(x - 3)$
4. Shift up by 1 unit → $-2f(x - 3) + 1$

**SAT Application:** Transformation questions ask you to:
- Identify the transformation from an equation
- Write the equation given a transformation description
- Determine how a transformation affects a specific point on the graph
- Identify the graph of a transformed function

### 3.7 Key Function Types on the SAT

#### Linear Functions: $f(x) = mx + b$
- Graph: straight line
- Constant rate of change (slope)
- Domain: all real numbers (unless restricted)
- Range: all real numbers (unless constant)

#### Quadratic Functions: $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$
- Graph: parabola
- Vertex at $(h, k)$
- Axis of symmetry: $x = h$
- Domain: all real numbers
- Range: $[k, \infty)$ if $a > 0$; $(-\infty, k]$ if $a < 0$

#### Absolute Value Functions: $f(x) = a|x - h| + k$
- Graph: V-shaped
- Vertex at $(h, k)$
- Domain: all real numbers
- Range: $[k, \infty)$ if $a > 0$; $(-\infty, k]$ if $a < 0$

#### Exponential Functions: $f(x) = a \cdot b^x$
- Graph: J-shaped (growth) or decreasing curve (decay)
- Horizontal asymptote: $y = 0$ (unless vertically shifted)
- If $b > 1$: exponential growth
- If $0 < b < 1$: exponential decay
- Domain: all real numbers
- Range: $(0, \infty)$ (unless vertically shifted)

#### Square Root Functions: $f(x) = a\sqrt{x - h} + k$
- Graph: half of a sideways parabola
- Starting point at $(h, k)$
- Domain: $[h, \infty)$ (unless reflected)
- Range: $[k, \infty)$ if $a > 0$; $(-\infty, k]$ if $a < 0$

### 3.8 Interpreting Functions in Context

The SAT frequently presents functions that model real-world scenarios. You must be able to interpret the meaning of function values, intercepts, and rates of change in context.

**Key interpretations:**
- **$f(a) = b$** means: when the input is $a$, the output is $b$. In context: "after $a$ hours, the temperature is $b$ degrees."
- **$f(0)$** is the initial value (the output when the input is zero).
- **$f(x) = 0$** means: find the input(s) that produce an output of zero. In context: "when does the object hit the ground?"
- **The slope of a linear function** is the rate of change: "the population increases by 500 people per year."
- **The vertex of a quadratic** represents a maximum or minimum: "the maximum profit occurs when the price is $15."

**SAT Application:** Word problems with function notation are common. The key is to translate the notation into plain English. If a problem says "The function $P(t)$ gives the population of a town $t$ years after 2020," then:
- $P(0)$ = the population in 2020
- $P(5)$ = the population in 2025
- $P(t) = 10,000$ means the population is 10,000 at time $t$

### 3.9 Equivalent Expressions and Function Forms

The SAT frequently asks you to identify which expression is equivalent to a given function expression. This tests your ability to:

- Factor and expand expressions
- Complete the square
- Simplify rational expressions
- Apply exponent rules
- Recognize different forms of the same function

**Example:** The function $f(x) = x^2 - 6x + 5$ can be written in vertex form by completing the square:
- $x^2 - 6x + 5 = (x^2 - 6x + 9) - 9 + 5 = (x - 3)^2 - 4$
- So $f(x) = (x - 3)^2 - 4$, revealing the vertex at $(3, -4)$.

**Strategy for "equivalent expression" questions:**
1. Simplify the given expression as much as possible.
2. Compare with each answer choice.
3. Alternatively, pick a value for $x$, evaluate the original expression, and check which answer choice gives the same value. (This is a quick elimination technique.)

### 3.10 Using Desmos for Function Problems

Desmos is invaluable for function-related SAT questions:

- **Graphing functions:** Type any function and see its graph instantly.
- **Finding zeros/x-intercepts:** Click the grey dots on the x-axis.
- **Finding the vertex:** Click the grey dot at the turning point of a parabola.
- **Finding intersections:** Click where two graphs meet.
- **Evaluating at a point:** Type $f(3)$ after defining $f(x) = ...$
- **Checking equivalence:** Graph two expressions and see if the curves overlap completely.
- **Using sliders:** Replace a constant with a variable ($a$, $b$, $c$) and add a slider to see how the graph changes dynamically.

**Pro tip for "how many solutions" questions:** Graph both sides of the equation as separate functions and count the intersection points. This is faster and more reliable than algebraic manipulation.

---

## Part IV: The Intersection of Trigonometry, Coordinate Geometry, and Functions

### 4.1 The Unit Circle as a Function

The unit circle bridges trigonometry and coordinate geometry. The equation of the unit circle is:

$$x^2 + y^2 = 1$$

This is a circle centered at (0, 0) with radius 1. Any point on this circle can be described as $(\cos\theta, \sin\theta)$ where $\theta$ is the angle from the positive x-axis.

**Connection to functions:** The sine and cosine functions take an angle as input and produce a coordinate as output. They are periodic functions — their values repeat every 360° (or $2\pi$ radians).

### 4.2 Graphing Trigonometric Functions

While the SAT does not deeply test the graphs of sine and cosine waves, a basic understanding is helpful:

- **$y = \sin(x)$:** Oscillates between −1 and 1, starting at 0, peaking at 1 at 90°, crossing 0 at 180°, reaching −1 at 270°, and returning to 0 at 360°.
- **$y = \cos(x)$:** Oscillates between −1 and 1, starting at 1, crossing 0 at 90°, reaching −1 at 180°, crossing 0 at 270°, and returning to 1 at 360°.
- **Amplitude:** The maximum deviation from the midline (for basic sin and cos, this is 1).
- **Period:** The length of one full cycle (for basic sin and cos, this is 360° or $2\pi$ radians).

### 4.3 Coordinate Geometry with Trigonometry

Some SAT problems combine coordinate geometry and trigonometry:

- Finding the angle a line makes with the x-axis using the slope: $\tan(\theta) = m$, so $\theta = \arctan(m)$
- Using trig to find coordinates of points on circles
- Converting between rectangular $(x, y)$ and polar $(r, \theta)$ coordinates

**Example:** A line has slope 1. What angle does it make with the positive x-axis?
- $\tan(\theta) = 1$
- $\theta = \arctan(1) = 45°$

**Example:** A line has slope $-\sqrt{3}$. What angle does it make with the positive x-axis?
- $\tan(\theta) = -\sqrt{3}$
- The reference angle is 60° (since $\tan(60°) = \sqrt{3}$)
- Since the slope is negative, the angle is in Quadrant II: $\theta = 180° - 60° = 120°$

### 4.4 Circles, Trigonometry, and the Pythagorean Identity

The equation of a circle $x^2 + y^2 = r^2$ is intimately connected to the Pythagorean identity:

- For the unit circle ($r = 1$): $x^2 + y^2 = 1$, and since $x = \cos(\theta)$ and $y = \sin(\theta)$, we get $\cos^2(\theta) + \sin^2(\theta) = 1$.
- For a circle of radius $r$: $x = r\cos(\theta)$ and $y = r\sin(\theta)$, giving us $(r\cos\theta)^2 + (r\sin\theta)^2 = r^2$, which simplifies to $r^2(\cos^2\theta + \sin^2\theta) = r^2$, confirming the identity.

This connection means that any problem involving a circle and an angle can potentially be approached through trigonometry, and vice versa.

### 4.5 Functions and Geometry — Modeling Geometric Relationships

Functions are frequently used to model geometric relationships on the SAT:

- **Area as a function of side length:** $A(s) = s^2$ (area of a square as a function of its side)
- **Perimeter as a function of side length:** $P(s) = 4s$
- **Volume as a function of radius:** $V(r) = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ (volume of a sphere)
- **Diagonal as a function of side:** $d(s) = s\sqrt{2}$ (diagonal of a square)

**SAT Application:** A question might say: "The function $A(x)$ gives the area of a rectangle with width $x$ and length $2x$. What is $A(4)$?" This is simply $A(x) = x \cdot 2x = 2x^2$, so $A(4) = 2(16) = 32$.

The key is recognizing that geometric formulas are functions — they take an input (a dimension) and produce an output (area, volume, perimeter, etc.).

---

## Part V: Advanced Nuances and SAT-Specific Strategies

### 5.1 The Reference Sheet — What You Get and What You Don't

The SAT provides a reference sheet with certain formulas. Know exactly what is and isn't on it so you know what to memorize.

**Provided on the reference sheet:**
- Area of a circle: $A = \pi r^2$
- Circumference: $C = 2\pi r$
- Area of a rectangle: $A = lw$
- Area of a triangle: $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$
- Pythagorean theorem: $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$
- Volume of a rectangular solid: $V = lwh$
- Volume of a cylinder: $V = \pi r^2 h$
- Volume of a sphere: $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$
- Volume of a cone: $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$
- Volume of a pyramid: $V = \frac{1}{3}lwh$
- 360 degrees in a circle; $2\pi$ radians in a circle; 180 degrees in a triangle
- Special right triangle ratios (30-60-90 and 45-45-90)

**NOT provided (must memorize):**
- Quadratic formula
- Discriminant
- Vertex formula ($x = -\frac{b}{2a}$)
- Vertex form of a quadratic
- Distance formula
- Midpoint formula
- Slope formula (though this is fundamental enough that most students know it)
- Arc length formula
- Sector area formula
- Equation of a circle: $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$
- Sum and product of roots
- Trapezoid area formula
- Surface area formulas (except cube: $6s^2$)
- Diagonal of a rectangular solid: $\sqrt{l^2 + w^2 + h^2}$
- Diagonal of a cube: $s\sqrt{3}$
- Trigonometric identities (complementary angle, Pythagorean)
- Sum of interior angles of a polygon: $(n - 2) \times 180°$
- Exterior angle of a regular polygon: $\frac{360°}{n}$

### 5.2 Common Traps and How to Avoid Them

**Trap 1: Confusing sine and cosine.**
- **Prevention:** Always label the sides relative to the angle first. Opposite, Adjacent, Hypotenuse. Then apply SOH CAH TOA.

**Trap 2: Forgetting that horizontal shifts are "backwards."**
- **Prevention:** $f(x - 3)$ shifts RIGHT by 3. Remember: "what makes the inside zero?" $x - 3 = 0$ means $x = 3$, so the graph moves to $x = 3$.

**Trap 3: Taking the square root of $r^2$ and forgetting it's $r$, not $r^2$.**
- **Prevention:** If $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = 49$, the radius is $\sqrt{49} = 7$, not 49.

**Trap 4: Using degrees when the problem is in radians (or vice versa).**
- **Prevention:** Check the problem for the degree symbol (°) or the word "radians." If radians, switch Desmos to radian mode.

**Trap 5: Assuming a graph passes the vertical line test without checking.**
- **Prevention:** Circles, ellipses, and sideways parabolas are NOT functions. Only graphs where every vertical line hits at most once represent functions.

**Trap 6: Misidentifying the slope from standard form.**
- **Prevention:** In $Ax + By = C$, the slope is $-\frac{A}{B}$. Don't forget the negative sign.

**Trap 7: Forgetting to flip the inequality when multiplying or dividing by a negative.**
- **Prevention:** This is a classic algebra error. If you have $-2x > 6$, dividing by −2 gives $x < -3$ (not $x > -3$).

**Trap 8: Confusing the domain and range.**
- **Prevention:** Domain = inputs (x-values). Range = outputs (y-values). Remember: "D comes before R in the alphabet, and x comes before y."

### 5.3 The 15-Second Rule for Calculator Use

On the Digital SAT, you have access to Desmos on every question. But using it for everything wastes time. Apply the **15-second rule:**

**If you can solve it mentally in under 15 seconds, do it by hand.**

Examples of problems to solve without Desmos:
- Simple slope calculation from two points
- Evaluating a function at a small integer
- Basic trig ratio ($\sin(30°) = \frac{1}{2}$)
- Converting 90° to radians ($\frac{\pi}{2}$)
- Finding the midpoint of (2, 3) and (4, 7) → (3, 5)

Examples where Desmos shines:
- Finding the intersection of two complex equations
- Graphing a circle and a line to find intersection points
- Finding the vertex of a quadratic with messy coefficients
- Checking which of four expressions is equivalent to a given one
- Solving systems of equations with fractions

### 5.4 Backsolving with Functions

When a question asks "which of the following is equivalent to $f(x) = ...$?" or "for what value of $x$ does $f(x) = g(x)$?", you can often use backsolving:

1. Pick a convenient value for $x$ (avoid 0 and 1, as they can produce false matches).
2. Evaluate the original expression at that x-value.
3. Evaluate each answer choice at the same x-value.
4. Eliminate any choice that doesn't match.
5. If multiple choices remain, pick a second x-value and repeat.

This technique is especially powerful when combined with Desmos's table feature (Ctrl+Alt+T), which lets you evaluate multiple expressions at multiple x-values simultaneously.

### 5.5 The "Cannot Be Determined" Answer Choice

On the SAT, "cannot be determined from the given information" is a legitimate answer choice. It appears when:
- The given information is insufficient to find a unique answer
- Multiple values are possible
- A necessary piece of information is missing
