**Strategy:** Don't automatically dismiss this choice, but also don't select it too quickly. Verify that you truly cannot determine the answer before choosing it. Often, the information IS sufficient — you just need to find the right relationship.

### 5.6 Connecting the Three Topics

Trigonometry, coordinate geometry, and functions are not isolated topics on the SAT — they are deeply interconnected:

- **Trigonometry + Coordinate Geometry:** The unit circle places trig functions on the coordinate plane. The equation $x^2 + y^2 = 1$ is both a circle equation and the Pythagorean identity.
- **Functions + Coordinate Geometry:** Every function has a graph on the coordinate plane. The distance formula, midpoint formula, and slope formula are all functions of coordinates.
- **Trigonometry + Functions:** Sine and cosine are functions that take angles and produce ratios. Their graphs are periodic waves on the coordinate plane.

**The SAT's hardest questions** often combine all three. For example:
- "A circle with center (0, 0) and radius 5 passes through the point (3, $y$). If the angle formed by the radius to this point and the positive x-axis is $\theta$, what is $\sin(\theta)$?"
  - Solution: The point $(3, y)$ is on the circle $x^2 + y^2 = 25$, so $9 + y^2 = 25$, giving $y^2 = 16$, so $y = 4$ (assuming the point is in Quadrant I). Then $\sin(\theta) = \frac{y}{r} = \frac{4}{5}$.

This single question tests the equation of a circle (coordinate geometry), the definition of sine (trigonometry), and the concept of a function (the sine function).

---

## Part VI: Comprehensive Formula Reference for This Chapter

### Trigonometry

$$\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$$

$$\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$$

$$\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}}$$

$$\sin^2(\theta) + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$$

$$\sin(x°) = \cos(90° - x°)$$

$$\text{Degrees to radians: multiply by } \frac{\pi}{180}$$

$$\text{Radians to degrees: multiply by } \frac{180}{\pi}$$

### Coordinate Geometry

$$\text{Slope: } m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$$

$$\text{Distance: } d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$$

$$\text{Midpoint: } M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$$

$$\text{Circle: } (x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

$$\text{Parallel lines: } m_1 = m_2$$

$$\text{Perpendicular lines: } m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1$$

### Functions

$$\text{Composition: } f(g(x)) \text{ means apply } g \text{ first, then } f$$

$$\text{Vertical line test: a graph is a function iff no vertical line hits it more than once}$$

$$\text{Domain: all valid inputs (x-values)}$$

$$\text{Range: all possible outputs (y-values)}$$

$$\text{Transformation: } y = a \cdot f(b(x - h)) + k$$
- $h$ = horizontal shift (opposite direction)
- $k$ = vertical shift
- $a$ = vertical stretch/compression and x-axis reflection
- $b$ = horizontal stretch/compression and y-axis reflection

---

## Part VII: Final Strategic Summary

### For Trigonometry Problems:
1. Label the sides: Opposite, Adjacent, Hypotenuse (relative to the given angle).
2. Choose the right ratio: SOH CAH TOA.
3. Remember the complementary angle identity: $\sin(x) = \cos(90° − x)$.
4. Know the exact values for 30°, 45°, and 60°.
5. Check whether the problem uses degrees or radians.
6. Use the Pythagorean identity when you know one trig value and need another.

### For Coordinate Geometry Problems:
1. Write down the coordinates of all given points.
2. Apply the correct formula: distance, slope, or midpoint.
3. For circle equations, identify the center $(h, k)$ and radius $r$ carefully (remember the signs and $r^2$ vs. $r$).
4. Check if lines are parallel (same slope) or perpendicular (negative reciprocal slopes).
5. Use Desmos to graph and verify.

### For Function Problems:
1. Read the function notation carefully. $f(3)$ means substitute 3 for $x$.
2. For composition, work from the inside out.
3. For transformations, remember: horizontal changes are "backwards."
4. For domain, look for denominators (≠ 0) and square roots (≥ 0).
5. For "wacky functions," follow the given definition exactly.
6. Use Desmos to graph, evaluate, and check equivalence.

### General Principles:
1. **Draw a diagram** whenever possible. Visual representation prevents errors.
2. **Label everything.** Write down what you know and what you're looking for.
3. **Check your units.** Degrees vs. radians, inches vs. feet.
4. **Use Desmos strategically.** It's powerful but not always necessary.
5. **Backsolve when stuck.** Plug answer choices into the problem.
6. **Eliminate wrong answers.** Even eliminating one or two choices significantly improves your odds.
7. **Never leave a question blank.** There is no penalty for guessing.

---

This chapter has covered the three interconnected domains of trigonometry, coordinate geometry, and functions with the depth and rigor required for SAT mastery. The key to excellence is not merely memorizing formulas but understanding the relationships between these domains and practicing their application until the strategies become second nature. Every formula, every identity, and every technique discussed here has been tested on real SAT exams and will continue to appear in various forms on future tests. Mastery of these topics — which together account for a significant portion of the SAT Math score — is essential for achieving a top score.

---


# Chapter 8: Essential Formulas Reference — What Is Provided vs. What Must Be Memorized

---

## 8.1 — The Critical Distinction: Provided vs. Memorized

The Digital SAT provides a **Reference Sheet** within the Bluebook testing application (accessible by clicking the $x^2$ icon at any point during the test). This sheet contains a handful of commonly used formulas. However—and this is one of the most strategically important realities of the SAT Math section—**the provided reference sheet covers only a fraction of the formulas you will actually need to answer every question efficiently.**

The College Board's decision to include a reference sheet is both a gift and a trap. It is a gift because it means you do not need to memorize certain foundational formulas. It is a trap because many students assume the reference sheet is comprehensive, spend insufficient time memorizing formulas that are *not* on it, and then find themselves unable to solve problems quickly—or at all—on test day.

Let us be absolutely clear about the strategic implications:

- **Every second you spend recalling a formula during the test is a second you are not spending solving the problem.** Memorized formulas are instantly available; provided formulas require you to click the icon, scan the sheet, and extract the relevant information. On a test where each question has an average of $95$ seconds, even a $10$-second delay per formula-dependent question adds up to significant lost time.

- **Some formulas that appear on the reference sheet appear in a form that may not be the most useful.** For instance, the sphere volume formula is given as $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$, which is the standard form. But what if the problem gives you the diameter instead of the radius? You need to know to halve the diameter first. The formula sheet does not tell you that.

- **Many of the most frequently tested concepts on the SAT are entirely absent from the reference sheet.** The quadratic formula, the discriminant, the vertex form of a parabola, the equation of a circle, arc length, sector area, the sum of interior angles of a polygon, special right triangle ratios, the trapezoid area formula, and many more are **not provided** and must be memorized.

The remainder of this chapter is organized into a comprehensive, meticulously detailed catalog of every formula you need, organized by content domain, with a clear designation of whether each formula is **PROVIDED** on the reference sheet or **MUST BE MEMORIZED**.

---

## 8.2 — The Official SAT Reference Sheet (Complete Inventory)

Before we discuss what is missing, let us first catalog exactly what **is** provided. The following formulas and diagrams appear on the official SAT Reference Sheet within the Bluebook application:

### Geometry Formulas Provided

| # | Formula | Expression |
|---|---------|-----------|
| 1 | Area of a circle | $A = \pi r^2$ |
| 2 | Circumference of a circle | $C = 2\pi r$ (or equivalently $C = \pi d$) |
| 3 | Area of a rectangle | $A = lw$ |
| 4 | Area of a triangle | $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$ |
| 5 | Pythagorean theorem | $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ |
| 6 | Volume of a rectangular solid | $V = lwh$ |
| 7 | Volume of a cylinder | $V = \pi r^2 h$ |
| 8 | Volume of a sphere | $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ |
| 9 | Volume of a cone | $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ |
| 10 | Volume of a pyramid | $V = \frac{1}{3}lwh$ |

### Constants and Conversions Provided

| # | Fact |
|---|------|
| 11 | There are 360 degrees in a circle |
| 12 | There are $2\pi$ radians in a circle |
| 13 | The sum of the angles in a triangle is 180 degrees |

### Special Right Triangle Diagrams Provided

The reference sheet also includes **diagrams** (not formulas per se) showing the side ratios for:

- **$30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$** triangle: sides in ratio $1 : \sqrt{3} : 2$
- **$45^\circ$-$45^\circ$-$90^\circ$** triangle: sides in ratio $1 : 1 : \sqrt{2}$

These diagrams show the triangles with labeled sides, so you do not need to memorize the ratios themselves—but you must know how to **apply** them, which requires deep familiarity.

### What the Reference Sheet Does NOT Include

Notice what is absent from the list above. There are no formulas for:
- Arc length
- Sector area
- Sum of interior angles of a polygon
- Trapezoid area
- Circle equation
- Any area formula for a parallelogram, rhombus, or trapezoid
- Any linear equation form
- Any quadratic formula or discriminant
- Any trigonometry
- Any statistics
- Any coordinate geometry (distance, midpoint)
- Any probability
- Any exponent rules
- Any percent formulas

This is a massive gap. The rest of this chapter fills it completely.

---

## 8.3 — Geometry Formulas: Provided vs. Must Memorize

### 8.3.1 — Circle Formulas

| Formula | Provided? | Expression | Notes |
|---------|-----------|-----------|-------|
| Area | ✅ YES | $A = \pi r^2$ | Know this cold; it is the most commonly tested circle formula |
| Circumference | ✅ YES | $C = 2\pi r = \pi d$ | Both forms are equivalent; use whichever is more convenient |
| **Arc Length (degrees)** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $L = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times 2\pi r$ | This is a proportion: the arc is $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$ of the full circumference |
| **Arc Length (radians)** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $L = r\theta$ | Only used when angle is given in radians |
| **Sector Area (degrees)** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $A = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times \pi r^2$ | Same proportional reasoning: sector is $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$ of the full area |
| **Sector Area (radians)** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $A = \frac{1}{2}r^2\theta$ | Only used when angle is given in radians |
| **Equation of a Circle** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ | Center at $(h, k)$, radius $r$ |
| **Inscribed Angle Relationship** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | Inscribed angle = $\frac{1}{2} \times$ Central angle (same arc) | This is a relationship, not a formula per se, but it is essential |

**Deep Dive — Arc Length and Sector Area:**

The key insight for arc length and sector area is **proportional reasoning**. A full circle has $360^\circ$ of arc and a circumference of $2\pi r$. If a central angle of $\theta^\circ$ intercepts an arc, then the arc length is simply the fraction $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$ of the full circumference:

$$L = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times 2\pi r$$

The same logic applies to sector area. A full circle has area $\pi r^2$. A sector bounded by a central angle of $\theta^\circ$ occupies the fraction $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$ of the full circle:

$$A_{\text{sector}} = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times \pi r^2$$

**Example of how these appear on the SAT:** A problem might give you a circle with radius $6$ and a central angle of $60^\circ$ and ask for the arc length. You compute:

$$L = \frac{60}{360} \times 2\pi(6) = \frac{1}{6} \times 12\pi = 2\pi$$

Notice how the provided formula for circumference ($C = 2\pi r$) is a **component** of the arc length formula, but the arc length formula itself is not provided. You must know to multiply the circumference by the fraction $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$.

**Deep Dive — The Equation of a Circle:**

The standard form $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ is tested on the SAT with surprising frequency, especially in the harder Module 2. You must be able to:

1. **Identify the center and radius** from the equation. If the equation is $(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 = 25$, the center is $(3, -2)$ and the radius is $5$ (not $25$—you must take the square root).

2. **Write the equation** given a center and radius. If the center is $(-1, 4)$ and the radius is $7$, the equation is $(x + 1)^2 + (y - 4)^2 = 49$.

3. **Convert from expanded form to standard form** by completing the square. If given $x^2 + y^2 + 6x - 8y = 11$, you must complete the square for both $x$ and $y$ terms to find the center and radius.

4. **Recognize that the SAT may present the equation in expanded form** and ask you to identify the radius or center. Completing the square is the essential skill here.

---

### 8.3.2 — Triangle Formulas

| Formula | Provided? | Expression | Notes |
|---------|-----------|-----------|-------|
| Area | ✅ YES | $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$ | The height must be perpendicular to the base |
| Pythagorean Theorem | ✅ YES | $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ | Right triangles only; $c$ is the hypotenuse |
| **Equilateral Triangle Area** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $A = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$ | Derived from $\frac{1}{2}bh$ where $h = \frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2}$ |
| **Heron's Formula** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $A = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$ where $s = \frac{a+b+c}{2}$ | Used when you know all three sides but not the height |
| **SAS Area Formula** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $A = \frac{1}{2}ab\sin(C)$ | Used when you know two sides and the included angle |
| **Sum of Interior Angles** | ✅ YES (stated as fact) | $180^\circ$ | The reference sheet states "180 degrees in a triangle" |
| **Exterior Angle Theorem** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | Exterior angle = sum of two remote interior angles | Not a formula per se, but a critical relationship |
| **Triangle Inequality** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $a + b > c$ for all combinations | Third side must be between $|a-b|$ and $a+b$ |
| **Special Right Triangles** | ✅ YES (diagrams provided) | $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$: $1 : \sqrt{3} : 2$; $45^\circ$-$45^\circ$-$90^\circ$: $1 : 1 : \sqrt{2}$ | Diagrams are provided, but you must know how to apply them |
| **Area of Equilateral Triangle (height-based)** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | Height of equilateral triangle = $\frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2}$ | Needed to derive the area formula if you forget it |

**Deep Dive — Equilateral Triangle Area:**

The area of an equilateral triangle with side length $s$ can be derived from the standard triangle area formula. If you drop an altitude from one vertex to the opposite side, it bisects the base into two segments of length $\frac{s}{2}$. By the Pythagorean theorem:

$$h^2 + \left(\frac{s}{2}\right)^2 = s^2$$
$$h^2 = s^2 - \frac{s^2}{4} = \frac{3s^2}{4}$$
$$h = \frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2}$$

Then the area is:

$$A = \frac{1}{2} \times s \times \frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2} = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$$

If you forget the formula, you can re-derive it using this method—but on a timed test, memorization saves precious seconds.

**Deep Dive — Heron's Formula:**

Heron's formula is one of the less commonly tested formulas on the SAT, but it appears regularly enough in Module 2 that you must know it. It is used when you know the three side lengths of a triangle but do not know the height and cannot easily compute it.

Given sides $a$, $b$, and $c$, first compute the semiperimeter:

$$s = \frac{a + b + c}{2}$$

Then the area is:

$$A = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$$

**Example:** A triangle has sides $5$, $6$, and $7$. Find its area.

$$s = \frac{5 + 6 + 7}{2} = 9$$
$$A = \sqrt{9(9-5)(9-6)(9-7)} = \sqrt{9 \times 4 \times 3 \times 2} = \sqrt{216} = 6\sqrt{6}$$

**Deep Dive — The SAS Area Formula:**

When you know two sides of a triangle and the included angle (the angle between them), the area formula is:

$$A = \frac{1}{2}ab\sin(C)$$

where $a$ and $b$ are the known sides and $C$ is the included angle. This formula is especially useful in trigonometry-heavy problems and in problems involving circles and inscribed polygons.

---

### 8.3.3 — Quadrilateral Formulas

| Formula | Provided? | Expression | Notes |
|---------|-----------|-----------|-------|
| **Area of Rectangle** | ✅ YES | $A = lw$ | Length times width |
| **Perimeter of Rectangle** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $P = 2(l + w)$ | Simple but not on the reference sheet |
| **Area of Square** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $A = s^2$ | Special case of rectangle |
| **Perimeter of Square** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $P = 4s$ | Simple but not on the reference sheet |
| **Area of Parallelogram** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $A = bh$ | Base times perpendicular height (not the slant side) |
| **Area of Trapezoid** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h$ | Average of the two bases times the height |
| **Sum of Interior Angles** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $360^\circ$ | For any quadrilateral |

**Deep Dive — Trapezoid Area:**

The trapezoid area formula is tested on the SAT with moderate frequency and is **not** on the reference sheet. The formula is:

$$A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h = \left(\frac{b_1 + b_2}{2}\right)h$$

The expression $\frac{b_1 + b_2}{2}$ is the **median** (or midsegment) of the trapezoid—the segment connecting the midpoints of the two non-parallel sides. The area equals the median times the height.

**Critical SAT Trap:** The height of a trapezoid is the **perpendicular** distance between the two parallel bases, not the length of either non-parallel side. If the trapezoid is "leaning," the height is not the same as the leg length.

---

### 8.3.4 — Polygon Formulas

| Formula | Provided? | Expression | Notes |
|---------|-----------|-----------|-------|
| **Sum of Interior Angles** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $(n - 2) \times 180^\circ$ | Where $n$ = number of sides |
| **Sum of Exterior Angles** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | Always $360^\circ$ | For any convex polygon, regardless of number of sides |
| **Each Interior Angle (regular polygon)** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $\frac{(n-2) \times 180^\circ}{n}$ | Only for regular polygons |
| **Each Exterior Angle (regular polygon)** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $\frac{360^\circ}{n}$ | Only for regular polygons |

**Deep Dive — Why These Formulas Work:**

The sum of interior angles formula $(n-2) \times 180^\circ$ comes from the fact that any $n$-sided polygon can be divided into $(n-2)$ triangles by drawing diagonals from a single vertex. Since each triangle has an angle sum of $180^\circ$, the total is $(n-2) \times 180^\circ$.

For example, a pentagon ($n = 5$) can be divided into $5 - 2 = 3$ triangles, so its interior angle sum is $3 \times 180^\circ = 540^\circ$. If the pentagon is regular (all angles equal), each interior angle is $540^\circ / 5 = 108^\circ$.

The sum of exterior angles being $360^\circ$ is a consequence of the fact that if you extend each side of the polygon in sequence and measure the "turn" at each vertex, you complete one full rotation—$360^\circ$.

**SAT Application:** A common SAT problem asks: "What is the measure of each interior angle of a regular hexagon?" You compute:

$$\frac{(6-2) \times 180^\circ}{6} = \frac{720^\circ}{6} = 120^\circ$$

Or equivalently, each exterior angle is $\frac{360^\circ}{6} = 60^\circ$, and since interior + exterior = $180^\circ$, each interior angle is $180^\circ - 60^\circ = 120^\circ$.

---

### 8.3.5 — Solid Geometry Formulas

| Formula | Provided? | Expression | Notes |
|---------|-----------|-----------|-------|
| Volume of Rectangular Solid | ✅ YES | $V = lwh$ | Length × width × height |
| Volume of Cylinder | ✅ YES | $V = \pi r^2 h$ | Base area × height |
| Volume of Sphere | ✅ YES | $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ | |
| Volume of Cone | ✅ YES | $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ | One-third the volume of a cylinder with same base and height |
| Volume of Pyramid | ✅ YES | $V = \frac{1}{3}lwh$ | One-third the volume of a rectangular solid with same base and height |
| **Surface Area of Cube** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $SA = 6s^2$ | Six faces, each with area $s^2$ |
| **Surface Area of Rectangular Solid** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $SA = 2lw + 2lh + 2wh$ | Three pairs of opposite faces |
| **Surface Area of Cylinder** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$ | Two circular bases + lateral surface |
| **Surface Area of Sphere** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $SA = 4\pi r^2$ | Exactly 4 times the area of a great circle |
| **Surface Area of Cone** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $SA = \pi r^2 + \pi rl$ | Base area + lateral area ($l$ = slant height) |
| **Diagonal of Rectangular Solid** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $d = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2 + h^2}$ | 3D extension of Pythagorean theorem |
| **Diagonal of a Cube** | ❌ **MEMORIZE** | $d = s\sqrt{3}$ | Special case of the above with $l = w = h = s$ |

**Deep Dive — Surface Area of a Cylinder:**

A cylinder has two circular bases and a curved lateral surface. If you "unroll" the lateral surface, it becomes a rectangle with height $h$ and width equal to the circumference of the base ($2\pi r$). Therefore:

- Area of the two bases: $2 \times \pi r^2 = 2\pi r^2$
- Lateral surface area: $2\pi r \times h = 2\pi rh$
- Total surface area: $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$

**Deep Dive — The Space Diagonal:**

The longest diagonal of a rectangular solid (the space diagonal) connects opposite corners through the interior of the solid. It is found by applying the Pythagorean theorem twice:

1. First, find the diagonal of the base: $d_{\text{base}} = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2}$
2. Then, use this diagonal and the height to find the space diagonal: $d = \sqrt{d_{\text{base}}^2 + h^2} = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2 + h^2}$

For a cube with side $s$: $d = \sqrt{s^2 + s^2 + s^2} = \sqrt{3s^2} = s\sqrt{3}$.

**SAT Application:** "A rectangular box has dimensions $3$, $4$, and $12$. What is the length of the longest straight object that can fit inside?" The answer is $\sqrt{3^2 + 4^2 + 12^2} = \sqrt{9 + 16 + 144} = \sqrt{169} = 13$.

---

## 8.4 — Algebra Formulas: All Must Be Memorized

**None** of the essential algebra formulas are provided on the reference sheet. Every single one must be memorized.

### 8.4.1 — Linear Equation Forms

| Form | Equation | When to Use |
|------|----------|-------------|
| **Slope-Intercept** | $y = mx + b$ | Graphing; identifying slope and $y$-intercept; modeling |
| **Point-Slope** | $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$ | Writing an equation given a point and the slope |
| **Standard** | $Ax + By = C$ | Systems of equations; integer coefficient preference |

**Key relationships:**
- Slope: $m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$
- Parallel lines: same slope
- Perpendicular lines: $m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1$
- $y$-intercept: the value of $y$ when $x = 0$
- $x$-intercept: the value of $x$ when $y = 0$ (set $y = 0$ and solve)

### 8.4.2 — Quadratic Formulas

| Formula | Expression | Notes |
|---------|-----------|-------|
| **Standard Form** | $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ | $a \neq 0$; vertex $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$ |
| **Vertex Form** | $y = a(x - h)^2 + k$ | Vertex at $(h, k)$ |
| **Intercept/Factored Form** | $y = a(x - p)(x - q)$ | $x$-intercepts at $p$ and $q$ |
| **Quadratic Formula** | $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$ | Solves $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ |
| **Discriminant** | $D = b^2 - 4ac$ | $D > 0$: 2 real roots; $D = 0$: 1 real root; $D < 0$: no real roots |
| **Axis of Symmetry** | $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$ | Vertical line through the vertex |
| **Sum of Roots** | $-\frac{b}{a}$ | Vieta's formula |
| **Product of Roots** | $\frac{c}{a}$ | Vieta's formula |
| **Vertex $x$-coordinate** | $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$ | From standard form |

**Deep Dive — The Quadratic Formula:**

The quadratic formula is arguably the single most important formula **not** provided on the reference sheet. It is tested on nearly every SAT, often multiple times, and appears in both Module 1 and Module 2.

$$x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$$

Every component of this formula is important:
- The **$\pm$** means you get two solutions (unless the discriminant is zero).
- The **$-b$** is the negation of $b$ (if $b = -5$, then $-b = 5$).
- The **$b^2 - 4ac$** under the square root is the discriminant, which determines the nature of the solutions.
- The **$2a$** in the denominator applies to the **entire numerator**, not just the square root term.

**Common errors to avoid:**
- Forgetting to make the equation equal to zero before identifying $a$, $b$, and $c$.
- Incorrectly computing $-b$ when $b$ is already negative (e.g., if $b = -3$, then $-b = 3$, not $-3$).
- Dividing only the square root term by $2a$ instead of the entire numerator.
- Arithmetic errors under the square root.

**Deep Dive — The Discriminant:**

The discriminant $D = b^2 - 4ac$ tells you the number and type of solutions without actually solving the equation:

| Discriminant Value | Number of Real Solutions | Graph of Parabola |
|---|---|---|
| $D > 0$ | Two distinct real solutions | Crosses $x$-axis at two points |
| $D = 0$ | One repeated real solution | Touches $x$-axis at exactly one point (vertex) |
| $D < 0$ | No real solutions | Never touches the $x$-axis |

**SAT Application:** "For what value of $k$ does the equation $x^2 + kx + 9 = 0$ have exactly one real solution?" Set the discriminant equal to zero:

$$k^2 - 4(1)(9) = 0$$
$$k^2 = 36$$
$$k = \pm 6$$

**Deep Dive — Vieta's Formulas (Sum and Product of Roots):**

For a quadratic $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ with roots $p$ and $q$:
- Sum of roots: $p + q = -\frac{b}{a}$
- Product of roots: $pq = \frac{c}{a}$

These are extremely useful when the SAT asks questions like: "What is the sum of the solutions to $3x^2 - 7x + 2 = 0$?" The answer is $-\frac{-7}{3} = \frac{7}{3}$, with no need to actually solve the equation.

---

### 8.4.3 — Systems of Equations Conditions

| Scenario | Condition | What It Means |
|----------|-----------|---------------|
| **No solution** | Coefficients of $x$ and $y$ are proportional, but constants are not | Parallel lines |
| **Infinite solutions** | Coefficients and constants are all proportional | Same line |
| **One solution** | Coefficients are not proportional | Intersecting lines |

In algebraic terms, for the system:
$$a_1x + b_1y = c_1$$
$$a_2x + b_2y = c_2$$

- **One solution** if $\frac{a_1}{a_2} \neq \frac{b_1}{b_2}$
- **No solution** if $\frac{a_1}{a_2} = \frac{b_1}{b_2} \neq \frac{c_1}{c_2}$
- **Infinite solutions** if $\frac{a_1}{a_2} = \frac{b_1}{b_2} = \frac{c_1}{c_2}$

---

## 8.5 — Coordinate Geometry Formulas: All Must Be Memorized

| Formula | Expression | Notes |
|---------|-----------|-------|
| **Slope** | $m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$ | Rise over run |
| **Midpoint** | $M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$ | Average of coordinates |
| **Distance** | $d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$ | Pythagorean theorem in coordinate plane |
| **Circle Equation** | $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$ | Center $(h, k)$, radius $r$ |

**Deep Dive — The Distance Formula:**

The distance formula is a direct application of the Pythagorean theorem. Given two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$, the horizontal leg of the right triangle is $|x_2 - x_1|$ and the vertical leg is $|y_2 - y_1|$. The distance is the hypotenuse:

$$d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$$

**SAT Application:** "What is the distance between the points $(3, -2)$ and $(-5, 7)$?"

$$d = \sqrt{(-5 - 3)^2 + (7 - (-2))^2} = \sqrt{(-8)^2 + (9)^2} = \sqrt{64 + 81} = \sqrt{145}$$

**Deep Dive — The Midpoint Formula:**

The midpoint of a segment is the point that is equidistant from both endpoints. Its coordinates are simply the averages of the endpoint coordinates:

$$M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$$

**SAT Application:** "The midpoint of segment $AB$ is $(4, -3)$. If point $A$ has coordinates $(1, 5)$, what are the coordinates of point $B$?"

Using the midpoint formula:
$$\frac{1 + x_2}{2} = 4 \Rightarrow x_2 = 7$$
$$\frac{5 + y_2}{2} = -3 \Rightarrow y_2 = -11$$

So point $B$ is at $(7, -11)$.

---

## 8.6 — Trigonometry Formulas: All Must Be Memorized

| Formula | Expression | Notes |
|---------|-----------|-------|
| **Sine** | $\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$ | SOH |
| **Cosine** | $\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$ | CAH |
| **Tangent** | $\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}}$ | TOA |
| **Cosecant** | $\csc(\theta) = \frac{1}{\sin(\theta)}$ | Reciprocal of sine |
| **Secant** | $\sec(\theta) = \frac{1}{\cos(\theta)}$ | Reciprocal of cosine |
| **Cotangent** | $\cot(\theta) = \frac{1}{\tan(\theta)}$ | Reciprocal of tangent |
| **Cofunction Identity** | $\sin(x) = \cos(90^\circ - x)$ | Complementary angles |
| **Pythagorean Identity** | $\sin^2\theta + \cos^2\theta = 1$ | Fundamental identity |
| **Degrees to Radians** | Multiply by $\frac{\pi}{180}$ | Conversion |
| **Radians to Degrees** | Multiply by $\frac{180}{\pi}$ | Conversion |

**Deep Dive — SOH CAH TOA:**

The three primary trigonometric ratios apply only to **right triangles**. For an acute angle $\theta$ in a right triangle:

- $\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{length of side opposite } \theta}{\text{length of hypotenuse}}$
- $\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{length of side adjacent to } \theta}{\text{length of hypotenuse}}$
- $\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{length of side opposite } \theta}{\text{length of side adjacent to } \theta}$

**Critical note:** The "opposite" and "adjacent" labels depend on which acute angle you are using. A side that is opposite to angle $A$ is adjacent to angle $B$ (in a right triangle with acute angles $A$ and $B$).

**Deep Dive — The Cofunction Identity:**

In a right triangle, the two acute angles sum to $90^\circ$. If the angles are $A$ and $B$, then $A + B = 90^\circ$, so $B = 90^\circ - A$. The key identity is:

$$\sin(A) = \cos(90^\circ - A) = \cos(B)$$

This makes geometric sense: the side opposite angle $A$ is the side adjacent to angle $B$, and both are divided by the same hypotenuse.

**SAT Application:** "In right triangle $ABC$ with right angle at $C$, if $\sin(A) = \frac{3}{5}$, what is $\cos(B)$?" Since $A + B = 90^\circ$, we have $\cos(B) = \sin(A) = \frac{3}{5}$.

**Deep Dive — The Pythagorean Identity:**

$$\sin^2\theta + \cos^2\theta = 1$$

This identity follows directly from the Pythagorean theorem. In a right triangle with hypotenuse $c$, opposite side $a$, and adjacent side $b$:

$$\sin^2\theta + \cos^2\theta = \frac{a^2}{c^2} + \frac{b^2}{c^2} = \frac{a^2 + b^2}{c^2} = \frac{c^2}{c^2} = 1$$

**SAT Application:** "If $\sin(\theta) = \frac{12}{13}$ and $\theta$ is acute, what is $\cos(\theta)$?"

$$\cos^2\theta = 1 - \sin^2\theta = 1 - \frac{144}{169} = \frac{25}{169}$$
$$\cos\theta = \frac{5}{13}$$

---

## 8.7 — Statistics and Data Analysis Formulas: All Must Be Memorized

| Formula/Concept | Expression/Rule | Notes |
|----------------|----------------|-------|
| **Mean (Average)** | $\bar{x} = \frac{\sum x_i}{n}$ | Sum of values divided by count; sensitive to outliers |
| **Mean with Frequency Table** | $\bar{x} = \frac{\sum x_i f_i}{\sum f_i}$ | Weighted average |
| **Class Average (Different Groups)** | $\frac{n_1\bar{x}_1 + n_2\bar{x}_2}{n_1 + n_2}$ | Weighted by group size |
| **Median** | Middle value when data is ordered | 50th percentile; resistant to outliers |
| **Mode** | Most frequently occurring value | There can be multiple modes |
| **Range** | $\text{Max} - \text{Min}$ | Simple measure of spread |
| **IQR** | $Q_3 - Q_1$ | Interquartile range; resistant to outliers |
| **Standard Deviation** | (No formula needed for SAT) | Measures spread; higher = more spread out |
| **Probability** | $P(E) = \frac{\text{Favorable outcomes}}{\text{Total outcomes}}$ | Basic probability |
| **Percent Change** | $\frac{\text{New} - \text{Old}}{\text{Old}} \times 100\%$ | Can be positive or negative |
| **Percent Difference** | $\frac{|\text{Difference}|}{\text{Standard}} \times 100\%$ | Always positive; uses the standard as denominator |

**Deep Dive — Mean vs. Sensitivity to Outliers:**

The mean is the most commonly used measure of center, but it is **sensitive to outliers**. If a data set is $\{2, 3, 4, 5, 6\}$, the mean is $4$ and the median is $4$. But if we change the $6$ to $100$, the data set becomes $\{2, 3, 4, 5, 100\}$, the mean becomes $22.8$, while the median remains $4$. The mean was dramatically affected by the outlier; the median was not.

**SAT Application:** "The average of five numbers is $20$. One of the numbers is changed from $10$ to $60$. What is the new average?" The original sum is $5 \times 20 = 100$. The new sum is $100 - 10 + 60 = 150$. The new average is $150 / 5 = 30$.

**Deep Dive — Standard Deviation:**

You do **not** need to compute the standard deviation on the SAT (the formula is not tested). However, you need to **understand** what it measures:

- **Standard deviation measures the spread or dispersion of data points around the mean.**
- A **larger** standard deviation means the data is more spread out.
- A **smaller** standard deviation means the data is more clustered around the mean.
- Standard deviation is **not affected** by adding or subtracting a constant from every data point (shifting the data).
- Standard deviation **is affected** by multiplying or dividing every data point by a constant (scaling the data).

**SAT Application (conceptual):** "Two data sets have the same mean. Data Set A has a standard deviation of $2$, and Data Set B has a standard deviation of $8$. Which data set has values more spread out?" Answer: Data Set B.

---

## 8.8 — Exponent Rules: All Must Be Memorized

| Rule | Expression | Example |
|------|-----------|---------|
| **Product Rule** | $a^m \times a^n = a^{m+n}$ | $x^3 \times x^5 = x^8$ |
| **Quotient Rule** | $a^m \div a^n = a^{m-n}$ | $x^7 \div x^2 = x^5$ |
| **Power Rule** | $(a^m)^n = a^{mn}$ | $(x^3)^4 = x^{12}$ |
| **Zero Exponent** | $a^0 = 1$ (for $a \neq 0$) | $5^0 = 1$ |
| **Negative Exponent** | $a^{-n} = \frac{1}{a^n}$ | $x^{-3} = \frac{1}{x^3}$ |
| **Fractional Exponent** | $a^{m/n} = \sqrt[n]{a^m}$ | $x^{2/3} = \sqrt[3]{x^2}$ |
| **Product of Radicals** | $\sqrt{a} \times \sqrt{b} = \sqrt{ab}$ | $\sqrt{2} \times \sqrt{3} = \sqrt{6}$ |
| **Quotient of Radicals** | $\frac{\sqrt{a}}{\sqrt{b}} = \sqrt{\frac{a}{b}}$ | $\frac{\sqrt{6}}{\sqrt{2}} = \sqrt{3}$ |

**Deep Dive — Common Exponent Traps:**

1. **$a^m + a^n \neq a^{m+n}$**: You cannot add exponents when adding terms. $x^2 + x^3$ cannot be simplified using exponent rules.

2. **$-a^n \neq (-a)^n$**: $-3^2 = -(3^2) = -9$, but $(-3)^2 = 9$. The parentheses matter enormously.

3. **$a^{-n} \neq -a^n$**: A negative exponent means reciprocal, not negative value. $2^{-3} = \frac{1}{8}$, not $-8$.

4. **$(a + b)^n \neq a^n + b^n$**: You cannot distribute an exponent over addition. $(x + 2)^2 = x^2 + 4x + 4$, not $x^2 + 4$.

---

## 8.9 — Percent Formulas: All Must Be Memorized

| Formula | Expression | Notes |
|---------|-----------|-------|
| **Part/Whole** | $\frac{\text{Part}}{\text{Whole}} \times 100\%$ | Basic percent calculation |
| **Percent Change** | $\frac{\text{New} - \text{Old}}{\text{Old}} \times 100\%$ | Positive = increase; negative = decrease |
| **Percent More Than** | $\text{New} = \text{Old} \times (1 + p)$ | $p$ as a decimal |
| **Percent Less Than** | $\text{New} = \text{Old} \times (1 - p)$ | $p$ as a decimal |
| **Successive Changes** | Multiply factors sequentially | Not additive |
| **Simple Interest** | $I = Prt$ | $P$ = principal, $r$ = rate, $t$ = time |
| **Compound Interest** | $A = P\left(1 + \frac{r}{100}\right)^t$ | Amount after $t$ periods |

**Deep Dive — Successive Percent Changes:**

When a quantity changes by a percent and then changes again by a different percent, you **multiply the factors**, you do not add the percentages.

**Example:** A population of $1000$ increases by $20\%$ and then decreases by $10\%$.

- After $20\%$ increase: $1000 \times 1.20 = 1200$
- After $10\%$ decrease: $1200 \times 0.90 = 1080$
- Net change: $8\%$ increase (not $10\%$ increase, which is what you'd get by adding $20\%$ and subtracting $10\%$)

**Deep Dive — "Percent More Than" vs. "Percent Less Than" Asymmetry:**

If $A$ is $p\%$ more than $B$, then $B$ is **not** $p\%$ less than $A$. The base of the percentage changes.

**Example:** If a shirt's price increases from $\$40$ to $\$50$, that's a $25\%$ increase. But if it then decreases from $\$50$ back to $\$40$, that's a $20\%$ decrease. The percentages are different because the base changed.

---

## 8.10 — Probability and Counting Formulas: All Must Be Memorized

| Formula | Expression | Notes |
|---------|-----------|-------|
| **Basic Probability** | $P(E) = \frac{\text{Favorable}}{\text{Total}}$ | $0 \leq P(E) \leq 1$ |
| **Complement Rule** | $P(\text{not } E) = 1 - P(E)$ | Useful for "at least" problems |
| **Permutations** | $\frac{n!}{(n-r)!}$ | Order matters |
| **Combinations** | $\frac{n!}{r!(n-r)!}$ | Order does not matter |
| **Multiplication Principle** | Total outcomes = $n_1 \times n_2 \times \cdots \times n_k$ | For sequential choices |

**Deep Dive — Permutations vs. Combinations:**

The distinction between permutations and combinations is one of the most commonly tested concepts in SAT probability.

- **Permutations** are used when **order matters**. Arranging $5$ books on a shelf is a permutation because the order of the books matters.
- **Combinations** are used when **order does not matter**. Choosing $3$ books to take on vacation is a combination because which book is "first" or "second" does not matter.

**Mnemonic:** **P**ermutation = **P**osition matters; **C**ombination = **C**hoosing a group (order irrelevant).

---

### GEOMETRY — MUST MEMORIZE

| Formula | Expression |
|---------|-----------|
| Arc Length (degrees) | $L = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times 2\pi r$ |
| Arc Length (radians) | $L = r\theta$ |
| Sector Area (degrees) | $A = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times \pi r^2$ |
| Sector Area (radians) | $A = \frac{1}{2}r^2\theta$ |
| Circle Equation | $(x-h)^2 + (y-k)^2 = r^2$ |
| Inscribed Angle | $= \frac{1}{2} \times$ Central Angle |
| Equilateral Triangle Area | $A = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$ |
| Heron's Formula | $A = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$ |
| SAS Triangle Area | $A = \frac{1}{2}ab\sin(C)$ |
| Trapezoid Area | $A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h$ |
| Sum of Interior Angles (polygon) | $(n-2) \times 180^\circ$ |
| Sum of Exterior Angles (polygon) | $360^\circ$ |
| Each Interior Angle (regular) | $\frac{(n-2) \times 180^\circ}{n}$ |
| Each Exterior Angle (regular) | $\frac{360^\circ}{n}$ |
| Rectangle Perimeter | $P = 2(l+w)$ |
| Parallelogram Area | $A = bh$ |
| Cube Surface Area | $SA = 6s^2$ |
| Rectangular Solid Surface Area | $SA = 2lw + 2lh + 2wh$ |
| Cylinder Surface Area | $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$ |
| Sphere Surface Area | $SA = 4\pi r^2$ |
| Cone Surface Area | $SA = \pi r^2 + \pi rl$ |
| Space Diagonal (rectangular) | $d = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2 + h^2}$ |
| Space Diagonal (cube) | $d = s\sqrt{3}$ |

### ALGEBRA — ALL MUST BE MEMORIZED

| Formula | Expression |
|---------|-----------|
| Slope | $m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$ |
| Slope-Intercept Form | $y = mx + b$ |
| Point-Slope Form | $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$ |
| Standard Form | $Ax + By = C$ |
| Quadratic Formula | $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$ |
| Discriminant | $D = b^2 - 4ac$ |
| Vertex $x$-coordinate | $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$ |
| Vertex Form | $y = a(x-h)^2 + k$ |
| Intercept Form | $y = a(x-p)(x-q)$ |
| Sum of Roots | $-\frac{b}{a}$ |
| Product of Roots | $\frac{c}{a}$ |
| Axis of Symmetry | $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$ |

### COORDINATE GEOMETRY — ALL MUST BE MEMORIZED

| Formula | Expression |
|---------|-----------|
| Midpoint | $M = \left(\frac{x_1+x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1+y_2}{2}\right)$ |
| Distance | $d = \sqrt{(x_2-x_1)^2 + (y_2-y_1)^2}$ |
| Circle Equation | $(x-h)^2 + (y-k)^2 = r^2$ |

### TRIGONOMETRY — ALL MUST BE MEMORIZED

| Formula | Expression |
|---------|-----------|
| Sine | $\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opp}}{\text{Hyp}}$ |
| Cosine | $\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{Adj}}{\text{Hyp}}$ |
| Tangent | $\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opp}}{\text{Adj}}$ |
| Cofunction Identity | $\sin(x) = \cos(90^\circ - x)$ |
| Pythagorean Identity | $\sin^2\theta + \cos^2\theta = 1$ |
| Degrees → Radians | $\times \frac{\pi}{180}$ |
| Radians → Degrees | $\times \frac{180}{\pi}$ |

### EXPONENT RULES — ALL MUST BE MEMORIZED

| Rule | Expression |
|------|-----------|
| Product | $a^m \cdot a^n = a^{m+n}$ |
| Quotient | $\frac{a^m}{a^n} = a^{m-n}$ |
| Power | $(a^m)^n = a^{mn}$ |
| Zero | $a^0 = 1$ |
| Negative | $a^{-n} = \frac{1}{a^n}$ |
| Fractional | $a^{m/n} = \sqrt[n]{a^m}$ |

### PERCENTS — ALL MUST BE MEMORIZED

| Formula | Expression |
|---------|-----------|
| Percent Change | $\frac{\text{New} - \text{Old}}{\text{Old}} \times 100\%$ |
| Simple Interest | $I = Prt$ |
| Compound Interest | $A = P\left(1 + \frac{r}{100}\right)^t$ |

### STATISTICS — ALL MUST BE MEMORIZED

| Formula | Expression |
|---------|-----------|
| Mean | $\bar{x} = \frac{\sum x_i}{n}$ |
| Probability | $P(E) = \frac{\text{Favorable}}{\text{Total}}$ |
| Permutations | $\frac{n!}{(n-r)!}$ |
| Combinations | $\frac{n!}{r!(n-r)!}$ |

---

## 8.12 — Strategic Memorization Guide: Priority Tiers

Not all formulas are created equal. Some appear on virtually every SAT; others appear once every few tests. To maximize your study efficiency, organize your memorization efforts into three tiers:

### Tier 1: Memorize Absolutely (Appears on Virtually Every Test)

These formulas are so fundamental that failing to memorize them would be catastrophic:

1. **Slope formula:** $m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$
2. **Slope-intercept form:** $y = mx + b$
3. **Quadratic formula:** $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$
4. **Discriminant:** $D = b^2 - 4ac$
5. **Vertex $x$-coordinate:** $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$
6. **SOH CAH TOA:** $\sin = \frac{\text{Opp}}{\text{Hyp}}$, $\cos = \frac{\text{Adj}}{\text{Hyp}}$, $\tan = \frac{\text{Opp}}{\text{Adj}}$
7. **Pythagorean identity:** $\sin^2\theta + \cos^2\theta = 1$
8. **Percent change:** $\frac{\text{New} - \text{Old}}{\text{Old}} \times 100\%$
9. **Mean:** $\bar{x} = \frac{\sum x_i}{n}$
10. **Probability:** $P(E) = \frac{\text{Favorable}}{\text{Total}}$
11. **Midpoint formula:** $M = \left(\frac{x_1+x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1+y_2}{2}\right)$
12. **Distance formula:** $d = \sqrt{(x_2-x_1)^2 + (y_2-y_1)^2}$
13. **Exponent rules** (all six)
14. **Circle equation:** $(x-h)^2 + (y-k)^2 = r^2$
15. **Trapezoid area:** $A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h$

### Tier 2: Memorize Thoroughly (Appears on Most Tests)

These formulas appear frequently and are essential for scoring above $600$:

1. **Arc length:** $L = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times 2\pi r$
2. **Sector area:** $A = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times \pi r^2$
3. **Equilateral triangle area:** $A = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$
4. **Sum of interior angles:** $(n-2) \times 180^\circ$
5. **Each exterior angle (regular):** $\frac{360^\circ}{n}$
6. **Cofunction identity:** $\sin(x) = \cos(90^\circ - x)$
7. **Permutations:** $\frac{n!}{(n-r)!}$
8. **Combinations:** $\frac{n!}{r!(n-r)!}$
9. **Simple interest:** $I = Prt$
10. **Compound interest:** $A = P\left(1 + \frac{r}{100}\right)^t$
11. **Sum of roots:** $-\frac{b}{a}$
12. **Product of roots:** $\frac{c}{a}$
13. **Sphere surface area:** $SA = 4\pi r^2$
14. **Cylinder surface area:** $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$

### Tier 3: Memorize for High Scorers (Appears Periodically)

These formulas appear less frequently but can make the difference between a $700$ and an $800$:

1. **Heron's formula:** $A = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$
2. **SAS area formula:** $A = \frac{1}{2}ab\sin(C)$
3. **Space diagonal:** $d = \sqrt{l^2 + w^2 + h^2}$
4. **Cube diagonal:** $d = s\sqrt{3}$
5. **Cone surface area:** $SA = \pi r^2 + \pi rl$
6. **Radians conversion:** $180^\circ = \pi$ radians
7. **Inscribed angle:** $= \frac{1}{2} \times$ central angle
8. **Triangle inequality:** $a + b > c$
9. **Parallelogram area:** $A = bh$
10. **Sum of exterior angles:** Always $360^\circ$

---

## 8.13 — Common Formula Misconceptions and Errors

Understanding a formula is not the same as understanding it **correctly**. The SAT is specifically designed to exploit common misconceptions. Here are the most dangerous formula errors students make:

### Error 1: Confusing Area and Volume Scaling

When a linear dimension is scaled by a factor $k$:
- **Area** scales by $k^2$ (not $k$)
- **Volume** scales by $k^3$ (not $k$ or $k^2$)

**Example:** If the side of a square is doubled, the area becomes $2^2 = 4$ times larger, not $2$ times. If the radius of a sphere is tripled, the volume becomes $3^3 = 27$ times larger.

### Error 2: Adding Percentages Instead of Multiplying Factors

A $20\%$ increase followed by a $20\%$ decrease does **not** return you to the original value. The factors are $1.20 \times 0.80 = 0.96$, which is a $4\%$ decrease overall.

### Error 3: Using the Wrong Base for Percent Calculations

"$20\%$ more than" and "$20\%$ less than" use different bases. If $A$ is $25\%$ more than $B$, then $A = 1.25B$, so $B = \frac{A}{1.25} = 0.80A$. $B$ is $20\%$ less than $A$, not $25\%$ less.

### Error 4: Forgetting That the Hypotenuse Is the Longest Side

In the Pythagorean theorem $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$, $c$ is always the hypotenuse (the side opposite the right angle). It is also always the longest side. If you solve for a leg and get a value larger than the hypotenuse, you have made an error.

### Error 5: Confusing the Vertex $x$-coordinate with the Vertex Itself

The formula $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$ gives only the $x$-coordinate of the vertex. To find the full vertex, you must substitute this $x$-value back into the equation to find the $y$-coordinate. Many students stop after finding $x$ and either lose points or get the wrong answer.

### Error 6: Misapplying the Discriminant

The discriminant $b^2 - 4ac$ determines the number of **real** solutions. A negative discriminant means "no real solutions," which is different from "no solutions" in the complex number sense. On the SAT, "no real solutions" is the correct interpretation.

### Error 7: Using Degrees Instead of Radians (and Vice Versa)

The arc length formula $L = r\theta$ works **only** when $\theta$ is in radians. If $\theta$ is in degrees, you must use $L = \frac{\theta}{360^\circ} \times 2\pi r$. Similarly, Desmos defaults to degrees mode; if a problem involves radians, you must switch.

### Error 8: Confusing Permutations and Combinations

If the problem asks "how many ways can 3 people be arranged in a line?" that is a permutation (order matters). If it asks "how many ways can a committee of 3 be chosen from 10 people?" that is a combination (order does not matter). Read carefully.

---

## 8.14 — How to Use the Reference Sheet Effectively on Test Day

Even though the reference sheet is incomplete, you should still use it. Here is how:

1. **Click the $x^2$ icon at the start of each module** to familiarize yourself with what is available. Do this during the introductory screens, not during a timed question.

2. **Use it as a safety net, not a primary resource.** If you have memorized your formulas correctly, you should rarely need to consult the reference sheet. Use it only to double-check a formula you are uncertain about.

3. **Know what is NOT on the sheet** so you do not waste time looking for formulas that are not there. If you need the quadratic formula, do not click the $x^2$ icon—you will not find it. You must have it memorized.

4. **Use the special right triangle diagrams** as a quick reference. The diagrams show the side ratios visually, which can be faster than recalling the ratios from memory—especially under pressure.

5. **Do not rely on the reference sheet for geometry formulas that are absent.** The sheet has no formula for arc length, sector area, trapezoid area, polygon angle sums, or circle equations. If you need these, your memory is your only source.

---

Before test day, you should be able to write down every formula in the "MUST MEMORIZE" category without hesitation. Use this checklist to self-assess:

**Linear Algebra:**

**Coordinate Geometry:**

- [ ] Distance formula
- [ ] Circle equation

**Geometry (Non-Circle):**
- [ ] Trapezoid area
- [ ] Equilateral triangle area
- [ ] Heron's formula
- [ ] SAS area formula
- [ ] Sum of interior angles of polygon
- [ ] Sum of exterior angles of polygon
- [ ] Each interior/exterior angle of regular polygon
- [ ] Triangle inequality
- [ ] Parallelogram area
- [ ] Rectangle perimeter
- [ ] Surface area of all 3D solids
- [ ] Space diagonal formulas

**Circle Geometry:**

- [ ] Sector area (degrees and radians)
- [ ] Inscribed angle relationship

**Trigonometry:**
- [ ] SOH CAH TOA
- [ ] Cofunction identity

- [ ] Degree-radian conversion

**Exponents:**
- [ ] All six exponent rules

**Percents:**
- [ ] Percent change
- [ ] Simple interest
- [ ] Compound interest
- [ ] Successive percent change method

**Statistics and Probability:**
- [ ] Mean
- [ ] Probability formula
- [ ] Permutations
- [ ] Combinations

If you can check every box, you are ready. If any box is unchecked, that is your priority study item.

---

## 8.16 — The Relationship Between Formulas and Problem-Solving Speed

The difference between a student who scores $550$ and a student who scores $750$ on SAT Math is not primarily a difference in intelligence or mathematical ability. It is a difference in **fluency**—the speed and accuracy with which a student can identify which formula or concept applies to a given problem and execute the computation.

Formula memorization is not an alternative to conceptual understanding; it is the **foundation** of it. When you have the quadratic formula memorized perfectly, you do not waste time wondering "what is the formula again?"—you immediately begin applying it. When you have the arc length formula memorized, you do not waste time trying to derive it from the circumference formula—you set up the proportion and solve in seconds.

The SAT is a timed test. Every formula you have memorized is a tool that is instantly available in your mental toolbox. Every formula you have not memorized is a tool you must either derive (costing time) or look up on the reference sheet (costing even more time, if the formula is even there).

**The goal is not to know the formulas. The goal is to know them so well that using them is automatic.** This level of fluency comes only through deliberate, spaced repetition. Write each formula on a flashcard. Quiz yourself daily. Apply each formula in practice problems. Test yourself under timed conditions. By the time you sit for the actual SAT, these formulas should feel as natural as your own name.

---

*This chapter has provided the most comprehensive catalog of SAT Math formulas available, organized by content domain, priority level, and whether they are provided on the reference sheet or must be memorized. Master every formula in Tier 1 and Tier 2 at minimum. Master Tier 3 if you are targeting a score above $700$. Return to the checklist in Section 8.15 regularly until every box is checked.*
